DFZ is responsible for appointing the independent RAP/LRP monitor. DFZ will also select a
qualified third-party organization to undertake completion audits at the end of each RAP period. All
such appointments are subject to approval by the Lenders. Details of monitoring arrangements for
resettlement and livelihood management are explained in Chapter 14.
For the livelihood restoration activities, DFZ will be supported by MoLMEP, as well as MoA, the
National Social Investment Fund of Tajikistan (NSIFT) and local government departments dealing
with land, economic development, resource management, gender issues, etc. LRP 2 will include
information about staffing and resources in the ministries and local communities to support the
PAPs.


     5.2 Other Ministries and Agencies
        5.2.1 Bureau of Technical Inventory (BTI)
The BTI, which reports to the State Committee for Land Management and Geodesy, is responsible
for the preparation of technical passports for land plots and households.
        5.2.2 State Committee for Land Management and Geodesy
The State Committee for Land Management and Geodesy is responsible for the identification and
preparation of resettlement sites.




69
           5.2.3 State Unitary Enterprise “Nakhguzor” 53
This enterprise sits under the State Unitary Enterprise (Narkhguzor), and is responsible for carrying
out asset valuations in line with the THPs.
           5.2.4 Ministry of Finance (MoF)
The MoF is responsible for setting the budget for DFZ and paying compensation funds into SUE
Amonatbonk (via the Compensation Committee) for withdrawal by PAPs.
           5.2.5 Amonatbonk
SUE Amonatbonk makes compensation available in the bank accounts of compensated HoHs.

           5.2.6 Ministry of Labor, Migration and Employment of the Population
           (MoLMEP)
Livelihood restoration support is provided through MoLMEP for PAPs who register as unemployed.
Participation is based on self-reporting rather than pre-identification. The following livelihood
support measures are available:
     1. Transitional allowance (one-time);
     2. Job counselling to assist resettled PAPs to find job opportunities;
     3. Skills training;
     4. Preferential loan (50% repaid) or 3-monthly income support (PAPs may select one or the
        other but cannot access both);
     5. Small business loan (micro-loans – subject to business case); and
     6. Job fairs and assistance to identify job opportunities in their new location.


           5.2.7 Ministry of Agriculture (MoA)
The MoA is working with project-affected communities to fully restore the agricultural capacity and
food security of Tajikistan, compensating for the lost agricultural land and production due to
impoundment for the Rogun HPP. The Ministry is responsible for finding the replacement
agricultural land and pastureland, advising on crops, farmer training and providing seedling support.
This support involves consultations with the PAPs on replacement crops and trees, site mapping,
environmental services and agricultural extension activities. The land provided to resettlers for
agriculture is land that is suitable for cultivation but currently not in use by another household or
community. The MoA assists in preparing the land, including advising on types of crops which can
be grown, fertilization, irrigation, etc. The Ministry also provides guidance on the valuation of trees
and crops, though PAPs are able to complete crop harvests prior to resettling. Where possible,
PAHs are permitted to continue harvesting crops even after they have moved to the new
resettlement sites, until they need to cease because of impoundment/reservoir filling in the crop
areas.
           5.2.8 National Social Investment Fund of Tajikistan (NSIFT)
Launched in 1997 by order of the government and established in 2002, NSIFT has functioned for
20 years as an implementing agency for local-level development projects funded by donors. They
have experience in working with jamoats to finance local-level, climate-resilient, socio-economic
infrastructure that addresses priorities identified through a participatory village needs assessment
and decision-making process. They have also supported livelihood activities, such as training,



53   Full name: State Unitary Enterprise “Narkhguzor” under the State Investment Committee of the Republic of Tajikistan.
70
helping beneficiaries prepare basic business plans, providing tools and equipment, and mentor
support.
       5.2.9 Other Ministries of the Republic of Tajikistan
Each ministry is responsible for ensuring that public services and infrastructure at resettlement sites
are adequately restored, e.g., the Ministry of Education is responsible for schools, the Ministry of
Health is responsible for healthcare facilities, etc. This includes the process of transferring
teachers, medical personnel and others to the new communities and/or employing new personnel.
Ultimate responsibility for coordinating their activities in the flood zone and resettlement sites lies
with DFZ.

78
By law, the State Committee on Land Management and Geodesy is responsible for identifying
suitable land for resettlement sites. For the selection of new resettlement sites, each district forms a
site selection committee comprising of all district level authorities: Head/Deputy Head of District,
Chief Architect, District Land Management Committee, Chief Land Surveyor, Land Engineer,
Committee on Environmental Protection, Fire Department, Electricity Department, Water
Department, Emergency and Civil Defense Department, Agriculture Department of MoA, Migration
Services of MoLMEP and jamoats heads.
DFZ is responsible for developing and implementing the resettlement and livelihood restoration
program. They employ two main contractors, one for construction at new resettlement sites and
another for demolition of relocated villages. DFZ also employs the Forestry Agency, which carries
out land clearance works in advance of flooding, and another contractor who transports property of
resettled households to designated resettlement sites.
The land is requested through the local authorities and must be approved by the receiving jamoats
and host communities before resettlement can proceed.
Before land acquisition for the development of resettlement sites can commence, the site must be
approved as safe and suitable by the site selection committee and subject to required approvals
from appropriate governmental agencies (e.g. the Committee on Environmental Protection should
confirm that the site is not vulnerable to, and will not cause, significant environmental risks).
Site selection should also be aligned with central government planning for social and economic
development.
As has been the practice at Nurobod and previous resettlement sites, the district-level site selection
committee has conducted environmental studies to assess potential environmental risks and
impacts, including: water supply and waste disposal (solid, liquid and hazardous); geological and
land risks (e.g. earthquakes, landslides); climate-related risks and hazards; areas where additional
population will not cause strain on local resources and where there is agricultural and pastureland
of sufficient quality to accommodate displaced people in advance of the relocation of displaced
populations. There has been one instance (in Turzonsoda) where the amount of common
pasturelands provided was not sufficient for the resettled population and, in response, additional
land was provided. Additional common pastureland was provided in response to a request from the
displaced population. In another site, Sebiston, additional agricultural land for resettled households
is being sought outside the village, as there is not enough land in the village. Environmental
management plans and/or environmental and social audits are being prepared for the resettlement
sites in accordance with national requirements. Once the project becomes effective, ESMPs for all
cleared land (after demolition) and the resettlement sites will be prepared in accordance with good
international industry practice as set out in the project ESMP.
With regard to demolishing relocated villages, in accordance with national law and regulations54,
contractors tear down structures to their foundations, dispose of all solid and liquid waste, seal off
structures which contain polluting waste, such as petrol stations, latrines, etc., cut down trees and
bushes and clear the land so that the site is ready for inundation. The aim to ensure the reservoir is
as free of debris and pollution as possible.
DFZ is required to ensure that reconstruction of all public infrastructure (i.e., water supply, health
centers, education facilities, roads, electricity lines etc.) and utility services in resettlement sites is
complete before relocation commences. Construction of these facilities is carried out under the
oversight of DFZ in coordination with responsible ministries.




54 These are detailed in a manual for the contractors.

79
         6.2.2 Land Use Rights and Legal Matters
Resettled PAPs are given security of tenure at their new location and provided with all relevant
legal documents. DFZ’s Legal Consultation Department, is responsible for advising resettled PAPs
on all legal matters. This includes providing them free Land Use Certificates after completion of
relocation, advising them on their entitlements, dealing with any complaints that may arise due to
resettlement, and facilitating their application to the appropriate institution if they need further
support. For example, if there are PAPs identified without formalized land use rights, such as those
who have inherited their land use rights but have not formalized their inheritance, they are directed
to the State Land Cadaster to register and gain formal recognition of their rights.
DFZ and local governments work closely with PAPs to help them resolve any disputes about land
and property in a timely and fair manner. PAPs may approach DFZ directly or through the project’s
GRM, which has been strengthened to respond to and resolve complains more quickly, with
transparent record-keeping.


         6.2.3 Implementation Schedule and Linking to the Civil Works
All physical resettlement activities must be completed before DFZ takes formal possession of the
land required for the dam, reservoir, transmission lines, utility roads, and other project interventions.
This includes the payment of all due compensation, and the completion/provision of all replacement
housing and infrastructure at relocation sites. The resettlement schedule has been synced with the
schedule for each phase of the reservoir impoundment. All PAPs people will be resettled prior to
the commencement of any impoundment activities. Community infrastructure (electricity, water
supply, school, health facilities, cultural centers etc.) in the new resettlement sites is being built
ahead of the relocation. In those cases where the community infrastructure is not quite ready, DFZ
is coordinating with jamoats and relevant ministries to ensure PAPs can temporarily avail
themselves of educational, medical and other services in nearby towns and villages.
The five newest resettlement sites were approved in September 2024. These new sites will
accommodate around 923 lands plots (104.6 hectares).

7.        ELIGIBILITY
      7.1 Resettlement Policy of Rogun HPP
The key objective of Rogun HPP’s resettlement policy is to support PAPs affected by physical and
economic displacement, including vulnerable groups who maybe disproportionately affected to
restore their livelihoods and standards of living to at least pre-resettlement levels or to improve,
where possible55. The Project, in cooperation with several ministries, has provided a budget for
resettlement to mitigate adverse impacts on affected communities, with scope for adjustments due to
rising costs or changes in scope.
PAPs have been, and will continue to be, consulted throughout the resettlement process to ensure
their perspectives are heard, and their preferences considered where possible. Details on the
consultation process are set out in Section 10 and in the Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP).
The Government of Tajikistan and DFZ have adopted the following policy commitments:
          •   Carry out meaningful consultations with all stakeholders throughout the process, in
              particular with resettled and host communities and including vulnerable groups, in an
              accessible and culturally appropriate manner;
          •   Ensure that all private and public assets and services are fully restored to at least their
              previous levels and conditions at resettlement sites;
          •   Provide full compensation at replacement value in a timely manner for lost assets and
              support PAPs in restoring incomes and livelihoods;
          •   Provide livelihood support to re-settlers whose livelihoods have been negatively
              impacted, until such time as their income-earning capacity has been restored to at least
              its previous level;
          •   Provide transport to the new location of PAPs and their possessions;
          •   Comprehensively monitor all elements of the resettlement process until it is considered
              that living standards have been restored to at least pre-resettlement levels for the PAPs,
              in line with Lenders’ standards and Project and government policies;
          •   Pay particular attention to the needs and perspectives of women and vulnerable groups,
              and provide additional support as necessary to enable their equal participation in the
              resettlement process;
          •   Continue cooperation with MoLMEP, Ministry of Labor and other government ministries
              and agencies, as well as with civil society organizations (CSOs), the private sector, and
              other relevant entities, to identify and maximize opportunities for resettled PAPs in their
              new locations;
          •   All physically and economically displaced PAPs will be eligible for livelihood restoration
              support to restore living standards and livelihoods of people and communities affected by
              the Project to at least the same level as before the Project or improved where possible;
              these PAPs will be included within the Livelihood Restoration Plan (LRP).
          •   The effectiveness of livelihood restoration measures will be assessed by regular
              monitoring and adjustment; and
          •   The resettlement process will not be considered complete until livelihoods and living
              standards have been restored to at least their pre-resettlement levels.




55   World Bank ESS 5 Guidance Note, Objectives,
     https://documents1.worldbank.org/curated/en/294331530217033360/pdf/ESF-Guidance-Note-5-Land-Acquisition-
     Restrictions-on-Land-Use-and-Involuntary-Resettlement-English.pdf

      7.2 Cut-off Dates
A construction ban for the fixed structures has been officially in place in the flood zone since Project
initiation in the 1980s and was re-affirmed from 2011, with enforcement from the jamoats. This being
said, the numbers of PAPs for Phase 2 did go up, mostly due to births, as well as family members
joining existing households.
The realized cut-off date to determine eligibility for entitlement is defined as the period when
Narkhguzor finalizes its asset valuation, including trees, based on the asset inventory provided by
the Bureau of Technical Inventory (BTI) in the Technical Household Passports (THPs) Annual crops
are excluded from the valuation, as PAPs are given adequate time to complete the harvest even if
they have already fully relocated.
DFZ also collects information on the members of the household, including names, dates of birth,
marital status, occupation and income.
During the Phase 2 resettlement, different cut-off dates for the inclusion of assets in a household’s
compensation have been applied to different households and villages, dependent on when the BTI
and Narkhguzor undertake their asset inventory and valuation. The cut-off dates for RAP 2 are listed
in Table 7-1 below:

DFZ has been engaging with the jamoats and Heads of Makhallas to locally announce the specific

              appeal and the final date for to sign-off and accept the valuation; and
•     Following the final asset valuation, the total compensation package which will be approved for
      each eligible household.




85
8 VALUATION AND COMPENSATION PROCEDURES
        8.1 Legal Basis for Valuations
The main legislation governing the implementation of valuations, including in the case of mandatory
acquisition by the State, is the Law on Valuation Activities 2020, which mandates the following:
        •    Valuation is to be carried out by the competent state body, hereby Narkhguzor, in line
             with a signed contract between the organization requiring the valuation (the Customer,
             namely DFZ) and the competent state body (Narkhguzor). The Customer (DFZ) is
             required to pay.
        •    Valuations must be carried out by suitably qualified assessors from the competent body.
        •    Valuations are to be carried out in line with the National Assessment Standards of the
             Republic of Tajikistan.
        •    Two copies of the valuation report must be prepared; one is given the customer and the
             other is kept in the archive of the competent body. The name of the assessor must be
             included, and the report approved by an official seal.
        •    Valuations must be carried out independently and without interference.
The National Assessment Standards of the Republic of Tajikistan establish the provisions and basic
principles of appraisal and set out the requirements for carrying out an appraisal of the value of
immovable property, movable property, objects of intellectual property, as well as requirements for
appraisal reports that are used in appraisal reports.
Real estate valuations are based on the current market value of the property, restoration costs and
the cost of waste disposal and, if applicable, environmental remediation. Since 2011, when
resettlement commenced, depreciation of structures and assets was taken into account during the
valuation; this practice ended in July 1, 2024 in order to conform with good international industry
practice and the requirements of the World Bank and other international financial institutions
investing in the Project.57. The initial value of a real estate object is defined as the minimum price at
which this object can be sold or handed over. This value is calculated based on the market value of
the object and must consider all assessment factors that affect the value of the object. To get the
exact market price, the assessor uses three methods of evaluation at the same time, including
profitability, cost effectiveness, and comparability, where applicable.
Legal requirements for the valuation reports are:
        •    A precise and accurate description of the requirements of the appraisal assignment,
             indicating the date of preparation of the report and its number, the purpose and possible
             use of the appraisal, and an explanation of any permissions and restrictions that directly
             affected the final conclusions and value;
        •    Information, analysis, opinions and conclusions to be disclosed in a clear and accessible
             manner; and
        •    A valuation report of the asset is signed by both the assessor and the owner of the asset.


57 The Government of Tajikistan, in approving the Resettlement and Livelihood Restoration Framework, which complies

  with the World Bank’s Environmental and Social Standard 5 (Land Acquisition, Restrictions on Land Use and Involuntary
  Resettlement), as well as similar policies by other IFIs providing financing for the project, agreed that they would no
  longer depreciate the value of the assets. While the vast majority of PAPs reported to the RAP consultants and the
  World Bank that, even with depreciation, they had sufficient funds to build improved structures in the resettlement sites,
  they have been informed through the stakeholder engagement process that, if they believe they did not receive sufficient
  compensation, they can bring their complaints to the GRM. The independent RAP monitor will also examine the
  compensation during RAP 2 and report their findings and recommendations.
86
The valuation report has to contain, at a minimum, the following elements;
       •   the name of the asset;
       •   location of the asset of valuation and address;
       •   the justification for the valuation;
       •   the date of valuation and the period over which the valuation works are conducted;
       •   the date of report preparation and the number of the report;
       •   the register form of the submitted valuation report;
       •   the name of the client (DFZ) and the owner of the asset;
       •   the name of the assessor;
       •   the purpose and potential use of the valuation;
       •   the identification of the appraised property and interests in the appraised property;
       •   the type of assessment and a justification for its use;
       •   basic terms, restrictions and permissions;
       •   reference to the regulatory documents used that regulate the valuation activity;
       •   justification of the used assessment methods and forms;
       •   general information about the object of valuation, including the description of the object of
           valuation and the main results of the inspection;
       •   analysis of received information about the object of valuation, including review and
           analysis of market data;
       •   analysis of the financial situation;
       •   calculation results and accepted summaries;
       •   analysis of the efficient use of the assets;
       •   conclusions and results on the value of the assessed assets, which are justified for
           making the final decision; and
       •   coordination of the valuation results.
It is recommended by the law that valuations should be carried out no more than 6 months before
the transaction for which they are to be used is carried out, as per the national legislation. This has
not always been the case, and, where there has been a gap in time, DFZ has coordinated updated
household assessments.
     8.2 Methodology
A step-by-step approach is followed to determine asset compensation entitlements. The six-step
process for asset valuation and compensation determination is summarized in Figure 8-1 with further
explanation provided below the figure.




87
Figure 8-1: Asset valuation and compensation process




The first step is the asset inventory, where a ‘Technical Household Passport’ (THP) is prepared for
each household by the district-level division of the Bureau for Technical Inventory (BTI). The THP
shows all the assets of the household that form the basis of valuation. The THP preparation for each
household became a requirement after Project re-initiation in 2009. The BTI is the only authority
authorized to prepare, amend and approve THPs. The THP is prepared based on an in-person visit
by a BTI representative that is announced by DFZ prior to the visit. The BTI representative is
accompanied by the head of household (HoH), the head of community (raisi makhalla) and a
member of the respective jamoats. Each THP is signed by the HoH to certify that the THP registers
all the assets accurately. The jamoats member will also verify the HoH’s claims regarding their
assets. If the HoH is absent (e.g., working outside the country), they will be contacted to nominate
another household member to undertake the process, which is usually the spouse of the HoH. The
THP contains the following main elements:
       •   The name of the HoH, village in which the plot is located, and the names of officials
           carrying out the inventory;
       •   A detailed list and preliminary valuation table of the household’s assets, including
           buildings, their size and the materials from which they are constructed, outbuildings
           fences, and other fixed assets;
       •   A floor plan of buildings;
       •   A diagram showing the layout of the plot;
       •   Administrative costs; and
       •   Stamps showing the date of approval.


88
Trees and movable assets are not included in the THP. Before valuation, the THP is reviewed, and
trees are added to the updated THP. The THPs are prepared in accordance with standardized
computer templates, which can be sorted by various data points.
Once the THPs are finalized for all the households for a specific village, after DFZ’s notification, BTI
delivers them to Narkhguzor, which is the authorized entity to carry out asset valuation and prepare
valuation reports.
The third step is the ‘asset valuation’ process. When resettlement begins for a given village, the
THPs are reviewed and updated as necessary, and valuation is carried out by the SUERRE. This
review also considers trees, including both trees on the household’s land plot and fruiting trees
growing away from the plot but which the household can demonstrate are theirs. The trees owned by
PAHs are counted and mapped by the MoA and their productive value is assessed, including the
time it will take for replacement trees at the resettlement site to attain the same level of productivity.
Non-fruiting trees growing away from the household plot are excluded, as ownership cannot be
established.
Annual crops are not included in the valuation process for the following reasons:
       •   Agricultural land is state-owned;
       •   PAPs engaged in agriculture before resettlement have the right to apply for agricultural
           land use at their chosen resettlement site; and
       •   There is a long transition period between the commencement of the resettlement process
           and final relocation, allowing the PAPs ample time to harvest crops before they are
           required to vacate the land. Indeed, many PAPs continue to harvest crops and trees on
           the land they have vacated. Where this is feasible and safe, the DFZ has allowed for
           such harvesting to take place. DFZ has a notification system in place to inform PAPs of
           about the filling of the reservoir, with dates for completing cultivation.
Narkhguzor has a contract with DFZ, which is renewed annually, to determine which villages are to
be valued in a given year. This contract states only the names of villages and does not specify the
number of households, plots or structures.
As explained in sub-section of 9.1 above, `Legal Basis for Valuations`, the valuation methodology for
structures follows the Standard of Pricing, which is based on numerous decrees standardizing prices
for building items. These decrees, and the standard of pricing, are renewed annually. This takes into
consideration labor costs, machinery, and the price of materials. Compensation for trees is
calculated in accordance with requirements set out by the Ministry of Agriculture; it takes into
consideration the age of the tree, its annual yield in kilograms, the price of the products produced,
and the amount of time it will take to restore the tree. Non-residential plots are valued using the
same methodology as residential plots.
Tajik law permits depreciation of up to 30% of the value of fixed assets when calculating
compensation, and this law was applied during the resettlement process from 2011 up to end-June
2024. After that time, when the government approved the RLRF, they ceased depreciating the
assets. The calculations, the valuation of each item, the percentage of depreciation (prior to July
2024) and the final compensation amounts are shown in the valuation reports.
Once Narkhguzor finalizes the valuation reports these are provided to, and signed by, the HoH.
HoHs who wish to query the valuation of their assets may request a re-valuation from Narkhguzor
via DFZ. This re-valuation is requested via a written form available at the DFZ office in Dushanbe,
and is carried out by different Narkhguzor representatives, who were not involved in the initial
valuation. However, DFZ and Narkhguzor report that the valuation rarely changes unless errors in
the THP are identified, as the values are externally set through the Standard of Pricing. There is no

89
time limit on requests for re-valuation, which may be carried out even after the resettlement process
has been completed. If the HoH requesting re-evaluation is unable to access the form in Dushanbe,
they may contact DFZ through the normal grievance mechanism channels or local representatives of
DFZ.
When the HoH accepts their compensation, DFZ informs the Ministry of Finance on the
compensation amounts to be paid.
As a final step, the compensation payment is deposited by the Ministry of Finance into the HoH’s
current account at Amonatbonk. If the HoH is absent, he/she will be contacted to either nominate
another family member or appoint a legal representative to whom power of attorney will be given, to
receive compensation. If the HoH is deemed not competent (e.g., due to mental incapacitation) to
carry out the THP preparation and valuation process, a procedure to transfer this responsibility to
their next of kin is implemented.




90
9. ENTITLEMENTS AND COMPENSATION


       9.1 Eligibility Criteria for Compensation
In Tajikistan, all land is owned exclusively by the State. Unrestricted, heritable land use rights can,
however, be acquired by private individuals and entities.
Displaced PAPs can be categorized into three main groups for the purposes of establishing
compensation and entitlements:
       •   People who have formal legal land rights recognized by the Land Code of Tajikistan.
       •   People who do not have formal legal land rights at the time of the cut-off date but have a
           claim to land or assets recognized under the national laws or that can become
           recognized through a process identified in the RAP (for example, those who have
           inherited the lands they occupy but have not formalized their inheritance). It is DFZ
           practice to assist such people to formalize their land rights prior to receiving
           compensation.
       •   People who have no recognizable legal right or claim to the land they occupy.
All households to be displaced by the Project will be eligible for compensation, including those that
do not need to be physically resettled but lose access to assets, land, natural resources or other
livelihood sources. PAPs with formal or recognized land rights are entitled to compensation for the
land and assets they lose. In addition to the head of household, adult married children in a PAH are
entitled to receive additional land plots. However, they do not receive compensation for the
construction of houses or structures on these land plots. DFZ tracks the families who receive new
land plots and those who decide to build new houses on these plots. In most cases, this benefit is
received by married adult sons living with their parents, who form a new household with their wives
and children.
All of the PAPs who come forward are compensated for lost assets, even if they were illegal
structures, but the PAPs have to obtain a certificate from the court to change their status. DFZ
helps all of them with the paper work and the courts do try to move as quickly as possible so that
people can be compensated as they move to their new homes.
All eligible affected households are receiving compensation before resettlement takes place or
before land is used for any construction related works. This is detailed in Section 0 ‘Entitlement
Matrix’. To date, the government has financed compensation for houses and structures out of their
own account. This will continue, but the World Bank financed project (Sustainable Financing for
Rogun Hydropower) is providing an additional USD 24 million for cash compensation for houses
and household structures.
In certain cases, there may be difficulties relating to the payment of compensation, including the
following:
       •   PAPs are uncontactable after every effort has been made to contact them;
       •   PAPs have repeatedly refused multiple compensation offers consistent with the RLRF
           and this RAP, and refuse to come to a settlement; and
       •   Complex legal disputes related to asset ownership arise.


DFZ has established a dedicated account at Amonatbonk for compensating PAPs. Funds can be
held in the account until PAPs are identified, the compensation amounts are resolved (either
through the GRM or through the legal process) or if there are other legal issues which need to be
resolved. The compensation is then transferred to the PAPs’ bank accounts. PAPs can also accept

91
compensation while they are waiting for their grievance or court case to be resolved; if their cases
are successful, they will receive additional compensation, adjusted for inflation. During the second
phase of resettlement, DFZ has been able to locate all the PAPs (or their representatives (usually
spouses) or heirs). Where there have been disputes about compensation, all PAPs have accepted
the compensation set out in the THP while waiting for the GRM to consider their grievances.
Vulnerable Groups
The different vulnerable groups within the affected population are defined in Section 5.7 of this
RAP. The socio-economic survey and census describe the vulnerable groups and include numbers.
While entitlements – provided under Section 0 – are being disseminated to all PAPs, DFZ is also
following up with households to encourage them to report on vulnerable members of the household.
Those who believe they are in the vulnerable group will first apply to DFZ’s local representative for
screening and assessment together with the jamoat representatives. Once they are confirmed and
identified as a vulnerable group, in line with the definitions in the RLRF and RAPs, they are asked
to come forward to be registered in the jamoats to benefit from the additional resettlement and
livelihood provisions for vulnerable groups under the project, such as support for livelihoods (e.g.
agricultural implements, seeds, seedlings and animals), construction of structures at the
resettlement site, training and moving to the resettlement sites. DFZ is also coordinating with social
agencies to ensure vulnerable persons are receiving entitlements, such as cash transfers, food,
agricultural extension support and medical support.

9.3 Housing, Infrastructure and Social Services
Once the PAPs have received their household land, agricultural land (if requested) and cash
compensation for their assets, they are responsible for building homes and other structures on their
property, in accordance with local regulations and codes. The government is not financing housing
developments for the PAPs. The Ministry of Energy and Water is responsible for providing
electricity and water supply to the houses and other structures. To the extent possible, sewerage
facilities are being established in resettled communities, a considerable improvement from the pre-
settlement situation, where very few PAPs had access to such facilities.
The Ministry of Education is responsible for expanding/refurbishing existing schools or, where new
communities are established (such as Sebiston), for building new schools. The Ministry of Health is
undertaking similar expansion or construction with regard to medical facilities, including hospitals
and clinics. MoLMEP has established training centers in some of the key towns in the resettlement
area.
jamoats play an important role in catalyzing the community to establish other community structures,
such as community centers, cooperatives (e.g. agriculture, apiary), markets and mosques.
DFZ has been providing information to PAPs about the various services through their information
offices, town halls, meetings and written materials.

completed for PAHs by May 2025:

10 SUMMARY OF LIVELIHOOD RESTORATION MEASURES

This is a summary of livelihood restoration measures. Details are in LRP 2.
A LRP in line with World Bank OP 4.12 was developed for Phase 1 Resettlement in 2014 (hereafter
LRP 1). This was applied retroactively to PAPs who had already been resettled in the period 2008-
2014, as well as those still to be resettled in the remainder of Phase 1, which was declared
complete in 2017. The 2018 completion audit for Phase 1 confirmed that standards of living had
improved for resettled PAPs based on the socio-economic indicators assessed (land plot allocation,
housing compensation, quality and supply reliability of clean drinking water, availability and supply
reliability of electricity, distance travelled for health services, quality of medical services, distance
travelled to schools as well as quality and grade levels available, number of household members
employed, other economic activities and sources of cash income). However, re-establishing
livelihoods did take some time.
   10.1 Livelihood Restoration Measures Provided to PAHs in RAP 2 (targeted and
   universal programs)
The available national government programs to support livelihoods fall into (a) targeted programs
focused on specific groups, such as project-affected people (PAPs) being resettled under the
Rogun HPP or vulnerable persons, and (b) universal programs accessible to all citizens.

       10.1.1 Assistance to Rogun HPP PAHs (targeted):

PAHs are entitled to:
   • A one-time transitional allowance of 200 TJS for each Head of Household and 100 TJS for
      each member of the household.
   • For displaced PAPs losing wages from employment (formal and informal), cash
      compensation for lost wages equal to three months of previous income or minimum
      subsistence income, with the additional possibility to get training for alternative activities for
      a period of up to another 6 months, during which the same amount will be paid. This also
      applies to persons who had a different income stream and/or are seeking employment now.
   • Apply for a preferential loan of 5,000 TJS, half of which is repaid without interest.
   • Paid vocational training courses (stipend of 75 TJS/month).
   • Replacement of agricultural and common land (e.g., pasture, hay land) with land of similar
      size and quality.
   • Small business micro-credits.
   • In-kind support for agriculture, including seeds, seedlings, animals and equipment.
   • Additional additional assistance, if they are vulnerable. This can include social assistance,
      food, medical supplies and equipment, assistance in going to schools, medical facilities and
      other community services.


It should be noted that, while houses and structures are being constructed at the resettlement sites,
PAPs have been able to stay in their former communities and maintain their livelihoods, including
businesses, agriculture, employment, etc. This has helped minimize the transition time.

Across all four regions (Darband, Faizobod, Rogun and Danghara), the framework and approaches
to support the resettled communities are similar. While there may be some nuances in local
implementation based on specific regional needs or contexts, the overall system for helping the
PAPs is the same.

Various governmental agencies, including the Department of Labor and Migration, jamoats (local
governments), and various development organizations, are working collaboratively to provide
100
support to the PAPs, ranging from assistance with employment, skills development, agricultural
transitions, and business re-establishment and growth, across all four locations.

Targeted financial aid support (5000 Somonis in interest-free loans) is available upon application to
the Rogun HPP PAPs to start a business activity. More men have submitted applications than
women. Until December 2024, 72 people (50 men and 22 women) from the resettled families have
received preferential loans in the total amount of 360 000 Somoni to establish their small
businesses.

    •     As of April 2025, the transitional payments to households and preferential loans have cost
          TJS 3,697,200. Compensation for one-time transitional assistance wages has totaled TJS
          324,200. As of March 2025, compensation for lost wages totaled TJS 360,000.

    10.1.2 Assistance to Rogun HPP PAHs (through universal programs):
National programs provide the following support from which PAPs may also benefit: agriculture
support; employment; training; and assistance to vulnerable groups.

    •     Agricultural Support

Although agricultural activities have declined slightly since PAPs have moved to their new
settlements in some districts, they remain one of the key programs supported by the national
government. The program also allows female-headed households to create income by setting up
nurseries and vegetable and fruit gardens.

A specific program is rolled out under the World Bank-funded Strengthening Resilience of the
Agriculture Sector Project, where the Ministry of Agriculture:

    (i)    Procures and distributes emergency seeds, seedlings, planting materials and fertilizers
           to farmers and vulnerable rural households
   (ii)    Procures and distributes small agricultural machinery to simplify women's manual
           labour.
Beneficiaries (rural households) for the above activities are identified through the Farmers’ Registry
maintained by Agency of Statistics under the President of Tajikistan (TAJSTAT) and based on a
household survey that is conducted to identify vulnerable dehkan farmers, including women.67
Farming households of PAPs are thus eligible under this program but will have to be included in the
Farmer’s Registry maintained by TAJSTAT.

At a national level, the Ministry of Agriculture provides access to free seeds, seedlings and animals
(e.g. bees, rabbits, cattle, sheep, fish), as well as programs specifically for female-headed
households, disabled people, and the elderly. Interested individuals can apply directly at the
national offices in person, via email or by registering at the district level at the jamoat offices. Free
equipment, such as hand plows, is made available to vulnerable families. Female-headed
households with less than 5 hectares of land may also apply for support.


    •     Employment Programs by MoLMEP

The State Program for Assistance to Employment of the Population of the Republic of Tajikistan for
2023 – 2027 covers all cities and districts on a universal level. This framework provides access to
employment support, unemployment benefits, preferential loans, job fairs, and free vocational
training.

67 Indicative criteria include less than three hectares (ha) land plot and access to water for irrigation.

101
For the Rogun PAPs, the following types of jobs have been offered:

           ▪   Permanent
           ▪   Temporary
           ▪   Seasonal
           ▪   Public

The estimate salaries indicated by MoLMEP varies between 2500 - 5000 TJS per month.

On a district level, the jamoats assist the project-affected people in registering on the national
website and applying for district or local employment opportunities. It is a requirement that
individuals bring with their identity documents and educational certifications as proof of their
qualifications.

The Government of Tajikistan provides access to a country-wide employment database. The
database lists open vacancies; anyone can register and apply for these positions. The PAPs may
register as job seekers on a district level, and the jamoats will refer their applications to suitable
vacancies. This opportunity also allows the project-affected people to register for an unemployment
fund to help them until they manage to get a job opportunity.

MoLMEP has assisted the cities and districts of Rogun, Tursunzoda, Nurobod, Faizobod and
Dangara with the following employment support: 892 jobs were provided to males and 235 to
women. These figures arrive from the 1,634 families that were resettled from the flooded area of the
Rogun HPP from 2017 up to September 2024.

       •   Professional Re-integration: Individuals previously employed as teachers, doctors, and
           government clerks are generally placed in similar positions within the new resettlement
           areas as new schools, medical centres, and jamoats are established.

       •   MoLMEP reported that 104 people from families resettled from the flood zone of Rogun
           HPP received unemployment benefits totaling 128 thousand Somoni.

       •   New schools and health centers were given priority access to employment opportunities.
           Over a nine-month period in 2024, 37 job fairs were held in the cities and districts, which
           employed 286 people, including immigrants from the flooded areas.

       •   The jamoats provided direct access to seasonal work in the area of residence related to
           harvesting fruits, nuts, and vegetables. During the time period, with the support of
           MoLMEP, 283 people received temporary work, 249 seasonal jobs, and 155 people from
           families of immigrants from the flooded area were involved in public works.

       •   The jamoats also assist individuals who form part of their districts in applying for
           seasonal agricultural opportunities. The commercial farms publish openings on the
           national employer database and inform district offices of opportunities. For example,
           large-scale farms will do this when they need help during the harvest-picking season.

       •   Direct access to employment or income programs is available in the new area of
           residence, where 494 people have been appointed to permanent positions.

       •   Access to work abroad.

102
In the village of Chorsada of Lolazor district of Dangara, 256 project affected households are
resettled. As of December 2024, 257 PAPs were employed in various jobs, and an additional 121
individuals were engaged in seasonal work like harvest collection, cleaning, and processing cotton.

It was observed by the authorities in Rogun City, that resettlement has provided more employment
opportunities for women: In their areas of origin, women had limited job opportunities, but in their
resettlement area of Rogun City, women are finding employment as medical staff, teachers,
cleaners and other jobs, reflecting the increase in overall employment opportunities available to
women.

   •   Training

The training programs are universal, but the PAPs are entitled to receive a stipend to facilitate their
participation. MoLMEP reported that more females than males from the targeted Rogun HPP PAPs
received access to vocational training. However, the training was focused on the arts and crafts or
sewing sectors. MoLMEP reported 647 vocational training sessions during 2024 at their education
center, attended by 431 females and 216 men. From 2018 to 2024, MoLMEP provided TJS 84,584
in stipends.
The Agricultural Institute of Dushanbe has provided training to the resettled population on tree
grafting and growing crops, with a focus on sustainable agriculture and climate change adaptation.

The local branch of the Ministry of Agriculture in Faizobod has held meetings with
resettled individuals to explain the specific agricultural practices and conditions of the region, but
there are currently no structured or formal training programs specifically focused on educating
PAPs about advanced or sustainable farming methods.

The Ministry of Agriculture has rolled out national education programs and plans to use national TV
programs as a platform to train agricultural-based communities or interested individuals. This
national-based education program focuses on planting methods, management of different soil
types, climate change, grazing methods, and agricultural areas.

An Adult Vocational Training Centre supported by MoLMEP is offering training programs lasting 2-3
months. These courses focus on practical skills and trades.

Vocational and Technical Lyceums: These institutions provide training programs lasting from 6
months to one year, offering more in-depth technical education. Both the vocational training centre
and the technical lyceums offer courses that are both free (under the employment support program)
and fee-based, offering options to different people.

The Centre for Development of Professional skills in Dangara offers free vocational training and
skill development courses for the local community including PAPs.

The Vocational Training Centre in Faizobod has a vocational training centre that provides courses
in in skills such as:

       o Welding.
       o Bricklaying.
       o Construction.
       o IT and accounting
       o For women: cooking, and baking.


103
Nationally & Internationally Recognized Certificates: Upon completion of these vocational training
courses, individuals receive certificates that are valid both within Tajikistan and internationally.

Community-Based Training Initiative: In Faizobod, DFZ and MoLMEP are actively collaborating to
organize training courses directly within the community. DFZ has secured a suitable space provided
by a local resident (a room in their house), and MoLMEP’s vocational training team is preparing the
location to deliver training to residents.

In Danghara, the jamoat organize community-based training programs within the PAP communities.
These programs focus on skills like sewing, knitting, and carpet weaving.


   •   Support to vulnerable PAPs


The Ministry of Agriculture has a national program providing access to free seeds and seedlings as
well as programs specifically for female-headed households, disabled people, and the elderly.
Interested individuals can apply directly at the national offices in person, or by registering at the
district level at the jamoat offices. Free equipment, such as hand plows, is made available to
vulnerable families. Female-headed households with less than 5 hectares of land may also apply
for support

In addition to agricultural support, vulnerable PAPs can receive targeted training and other support.
MoLMEP has programs to support disabled PAPs, especially women or female-headed
households. The Ministry reported that five disabled women who were resettled in Tursunzoda
received vocational training in sewing at the Tajikistan Adult Learning Center. In addition, a list of
names submitted provides information on disabled adults and children under 18. These names
reflect the Nurobod district and are calculated to be a total of 75. In other districts, it was reported
that no disabled people had been recorded as receiving assistance.

Jamoats are willing to support the requests of vulnerable families, such as disabled people or single
mothers. For example, in Faizobod, the jamoat a shop was provided by the jamoat on the first floor
for a lady with a disability, making her sewing shop easily accessible.


   10.2 Additional Livelihood Support


   •   Stipends for apprenticeships/TVET (USD 2 million)

These stipends are intended to support unemployed men and women in the RAP 2 area while they
undergo vocational training/education to improve their skills and employability. This will thereby
enhance their livelihoods and promote their economic inclusion in their resettlement communities.
The skills training/education will facilitate their (re-)entry into the labor market and strengthen their
ability to engage in jobs and income-generating activities.
Stipends will be allocated over two years in proportion to the number of male and female PAPs
settled in each of the above-listed districts.
Through the project, DFZ will be providing stipends of 1000 TJS per month to people enrolling in
training programs, totaling US$2 million.


   •   Community investments for livelihoods (USD 17.5 million)

104
The Government of Tajikistan’s Socio-Economic Resilience Support Project (SERSP) AF is
designed to complement the Sustainable Financing for Rogun Hydropower Project.
The Additional Financing (AF) under the SERSP aims to strengthen socio-economic resilience in
Rogun-affected and disadvantaged jamoats through inclusive, participatory investments in
livelihoods and local infrastructure. By complementing the Government of Tajikistan’s ongoing
efforts, it will complement livelihood restoration, promote local economic development, and build
climate resilience through targeted investments, grants, training, and institutional capacity building.
More specifically, it complements the needs identified through community consultations done as
part of the ESF engagement including business trainings and grants to help set up agriculture and
tourism related enterprises. First, it will complement the livelihood restoration activities included in
component 3 of the Rogun Project. While the government is providing livelihood restoration support
to project-affected persons (PAPs) who are resettled, livelihood restoration takes time and
significant resources, and stakeholders have requested additional funding and technical support to
set up small businesses as well as small local infrastructure investments. The AF will support host
villages and jamoats and other communities with additional infrastructure investments and
livelihood training and technical support for individuals, groups and small businesses. Second, the
Government can use SERSP AF resources to supplement the limited revenues for Rogun HPP
benefit sharing that will be available in the early years of Rogun construction.
The AF will include Rogun-affected jamoats (70% of AF community investments, totaling US$17.5
million) as well as disadvantaged jamoats (the remaining 30%) in other parts of the country (using
criteria such as population, investments per capita, and unemployment), in agreement with the
Government. In the Rogun-affected area, the AF will cover jamoats in the five districts and cities
(Faizobad, Nurobad, and Danghara districts, Rogun and Tursunzoda cities) that include
resettlement villages under the second Rogun HPP Resettlement Action Plan (RAP2) – – 5 jamoats
and Darband small town with 8 resettlement villages and an additional 27 jamoats across the five
districts. This program is being managed by the National Social Investment Fund of Tajikistan
(NSIFT), which will work with Rogun PMG, DFZ and other agencies involved in the Rogun area.
The AF is providing subgrants to Rogun-affected and other jamoats to finance local-level, climate-
resilient, socio-economic infrastructure that addresses priorities identified through additional two
cycles of a participatory village needs assessment and a decision-making process. It will strengthen
the capacity of jamoat administrations and community-level institutions, including mahalla
committees, to engage with citizens and deliver services efficiently, fairly, and in a way that
responds to the needs of citizens, including youth and women. This component will finance public
goods, civil works, community mobilization and capacity building support for community members
and local government staff. Investments could include:
•      Rehabilitation or construction of kindergartens, schools, local health clinics, energy
generation and distribution, local transportation infrastructure and rehabilitation of village-level
water supply and sanitation systems, on-farm irrigation systems, energy.
•   Eligible subprojects could also include economic infrastructure, such as small-scale processing
    and storage facilities for horticultural products. The types of work may involve new construction,
    rehabilitation or maintenance. They may also include investments for nature-based tourism
    infrastructure or the restoration of degraded landscapes, involving nature-based solutions such
    as reforestation with local or drought-resistant species, nurseries, plantations and related
    infrastructure. Subprojects will integrate renewable energy and energy efficiency enhancements
    where feasible as well as support investments that strengthen resilience against climate-related
    impacts.

The allocation will be made per jamoat for social and economic investments and will average about
US$300,000. The funding in the participating districts will depend on the number of jamoats and

105
vary between US$0.60-US$2.7 million. Each jamoat will receive investment but it will vary up to
US$500,000 per jamoat, depending on evaluation and scoring of jamoat proposals.
In addition to the activities above, the AF will continue to finance mobilization and capacity building
activities both in the Rogun area and outside of it to ensure that: (i) communities, particularly
women, youth, and the vulnerable, are actively engaged in selecting poverty-focused project
interventions; (ii) village and jamoat committees use participatory, transparent approaches to
identify, implement and monitor local development projects; (iii) village and jamoats improve their
capabilities to prepare, raise funds for, and implement local development plans reflecting
community priorities. It will mobilize jamoats and community members to improve: (i) local
economic development, including preparation and implementation of the local development plans
and market assessments, along with provision of computer equipment and furniture;; (ii) climate
resilience by raising awareness of climate change risks, and promoting investments that support
adaptation and mitigation and enhance local resilience; (iii) women’s economic empowerment,
gender grassroots leadership and approaches to combat gender-based violence; (iv) participatory,
inclusive, responsive and accountable local planning, project implementation, and monitoring; and
(v) social accountability processes. In addition, if needed the component will include awareness
raising and outreach to inform communities about the and benefit-sharing support (BSP); and
capacity strengthening of jamoats to potentially manage the future BSP and related communication,
depending upon final BSP design.
The AF will also finance inclusive livelihoods with a focus on women and youth. The activities under
this component are designed to be flexible and to complement the Government of Tajikistan’s
livelihood restoration support for Rogun-affected communities as they become clearer for the
current Resettlement Action Plan (RAP) and the future RAPs. This subcomponent will target
community members, particularly women, youth, returning migrants and Rogun affected
communities who don’t qualify for more capital-intensive entrepreneurship support. Tailored
mobilization efforts, gender-responsive training modules, and outreach to returning migrants will
help ensure equitable participation of disadvantaged groups. The project will identify and provide
7,000 beneficiaries (individuals resident in the Project areas) with entrepreneurship and business
development trainings. These beneficiaries will also get hands on support to develop business
plans (as individuals or common interest groups). The project will select and support the top 1,600
applicants with tools and equipment in line with their business plans. Individual beneficiaries can
receive tools and equipment up to US$1,600 and groups up to US$10,000. Following the selection
of business plans, the beneficiaries will also complete additional trainings on topics such as
marketing, financial literacy, digital business tools, climate change and group cohesion. In addition
to the tools and equipment, beneficiaries will also be provided coaching and mentoring for at least
12 months that will include support with market linkages, registration, financial management,
amongst others.
In addition, the AF will support existing and registered MSEs in target districts as business partners
to improve local economic opportunities, create value chain linkages with downstream producers
and generate new jobs for women, youth, returning migrants and other beneficiaries. This
subcomponent will identify and support 100 MSEs through provision of core business expansion
trainings. Following completion of these trainings, the MSEs will be invited to submit a business
plan to secure grants from the project. These MSEs will also get hands on support to develop
business plans. The selection of MSEs for receiving grants will be competitive and on a rolling
basis. The project will support approximately 50 business plans from MSEs with individual grants
up to US$20,000 each that can finance tools, equipment, nature-based solutions and infrastructure,
consultancies and technical assistance needs. The coaching and mentoring will be tailored and
include support with business planning, registration, market linkages, financial management and
other topics. The project will actively target women, setting a 30% participation target for this
component, which will be monitored through the results framework.


106
The progress and quality of the program will be tracked progress trough robust M&E inlucding
baseline, midline and endline and feedback mechanisms (e.g., social audits, grievance redress
mechanisms, participatory M&E).




107
11     COMMUNITY PARTICIPATION

     11.1 RAP 2 Stakeholder Engagement Undertaken to Date
Since its formation in 2011, DFZ has been responsible for managing and coordinating the
resettlement program, including engagement with resettlement-affected communities. DFZ chairs
and arranges the community consultations by coordinating with the local authorities and jamoats
Heads prior to upcoming sessions. The jamoats Heads inform each household within the
settlement about the date, time and objectives of the meetings, which are held in large community
spaces such as mosques, tea houses or schools to ensure maximum participation from local
residents. This process is also supported by the DFZ representatives based in each district who
also notify people of upcoming consultations.
From 2017- May 2025, DFZ has led 38 community engagements with resettled, to-be-resettled and
host communities during the second phase of resettlement (there were no consultations in 2020
due to COVID). The consultations have involved community leaders, heads of households (HoHs),
other household members, including women, youth and elderly persons and various vulnerable
persons affected by the resettlement process. The focus of the consultations for the PAPs has
been on the process of doing the household census, preparing the asset inventory, receiving
compensation, preparing the asset inventory and selecting resettlement sites. Discussions with
host communities have focused on the influx of PAPs, impacts on resources and community
infrastructure/facilities and the attendant need to enhance, expand and/or construct new social
infrastructure. DFZ has also led discussions on social cohesion and encouraged host communities
to communicate regularly about issues of concern. The engagement log for this period is provided
in the Stakeholder Engagement Plan. Summaries of consultations are in Appendices E, F and G.
DFZ’s website also includes information on consultations related to resettlement: https://mmz.nbo-
rogun.tj.
DFZ follows up community consultations with smaller meetings involving PAHs and groups that are
planning to settle near each other, such as neighbors and family members. Active participation by
the PAPs or their representatives is required for resettlement site selection, planning and
construction of community infrastructure and amenities, and crop and tree assessments. Displaced
PAPs are consulted on a continuous basis throughout the resettlement process, and their views
taken into account as far as possible regarding resettlement locations, livelihood restoration,
mitigation of construction impacts, and preparation and location of resettlement sites. This is
essential to complete the resettlement process properly, maintain public acceptability of the Project
and good community relations, prevent misunderstanding and disinformation, and to identify,
prevent and mitigate negative impacts on PAPs.


         11. 2 Surveys of PAHs Which Are Resettled or Are in the Process of Being
         Resettled
PAHs yet to be resettled: According to the June 2024 survey of 1038 PAHs who had not yet been
resettled, 81% indicated they were informed about the project. Seventy-three percent of PAHs have
attended consultation meetings on the Rogun hydropower project, which includes a RAP; another
20% noted that they were aware, but did not attend the consultation; and 5% said they had not
heard of the project or the resettlement. About one third of the PAHs said they were informed
monthly about general resettlement issues; the remainder said they would receive information at
least once every three months – unless they were commencing resettlement, in which case they
were kept informed every few days. Seventy-eight percent of PAHs said they were satisfied with
the information provided about the project.
PAHs that have been resettled: Of the 363 resettled households surveyed in June 2024, the vast
majority of households (94%) noted that they had received information about the project, with the

108
remainder indicating that they did not know about it. In Dangara, 100% of the respondents noted
that they are informed about the Project.
With regard to resettlement, the vast majority of resettled households (80%) indicated that they
were aware of the RAP and had participated in consultation meetings. Seventeen percent of
households said they were aware of the RAP but had not attended consultation meetings. A
minority of households (2%) reported that they had heard about the RAP, but did not know what it
was about. One percent of households said they were not aware of the RAP and were hearing
about it for the first time.
Among the households informed about the Project, the largest share of households (45%) noted
that they received information updates every week, ensuring a regular flow of up-to-date
information. Another 27% received information about twice a month, and 21% said they received
information once a month. The remaining 7% of households indicated a significantly rarer
frequency of information: 2% received updates about twice every 3 months; another 2%, once
every 3 months; for 1% of respondents, information was received once every six months, and for
another 1%, once a year.
Almost half of the survey participants (49%) were completely satisfied with the information
provided, and another 46% said they were partially satisfied. Only 2% of respondents found it
difficult to answer, and another 2% expressed dissatisfaction, which may indicate the presence of
certain gaps in the information provided or in its perception. Most PAHs indicated that they fully
(81%) or partially (18%) understood the information provided. One percent could not
unambiguously determine the level of understanding or they considered the information was
partially incomprehensible.
Starting in 2023, DFZ has worked to improve significantly its outreach to PAPs, not only inviting
them to meetings and sending information to them but guiding them on filing complaints and
resolving outstanding issues related to resettlement.


   11.3 Future RAPs
In January-February 2024, DFZ held community consultations with PAPs in the Rasht District,
where RAPs 3-5 will be implemented. Appendix H summarizes the consultation to date. Future
RAPs will also include how information disclosure will take place, the channels for disseminating
information and consultations for the relevant RAP periods.
The SEP (which is available at http://www.energyprojects.tj) explains the process of engaging
stakeholders, including the PAPs, with specialized measures for vulnerable groups. The
engagements targeted at project-affected communities are listed below:
       •   Disclosure of the RAPs (online, posters and hard copies in affected communities, TV
           and radio broadcasts), alongside the ESIA/ESMP and LRP 2. This is being managed
           jointly by PMG and DFZ, through the Stakeholder Engagement Lead (SEL) and District
           Level Livelihood Engagement Officers (DLEOs).
       •   Public consultation meetings and focus groups for host communities, affected
           businesses and other affected communities within the Project area, to present RAPs/
           LRPs. This will be implemented by the DFZ DLEOs after disclosure, in 2025.
       •   Consultations associated with the RAPs, throughout the resettlement period as each
           new RAP is developed. DFZ is responsible for these consultations, which will be
           implemented by the DLEOs:
       •   Appropriate distribution of the RAP;
       •   Negotiation with landowners and users (formal and informal) to agree entitlements
           and compensation amounts, in line with the RAP;

109
       •   Continued consultation with PAPs to ensure effective compensation disbursement;
       •   Further consultation during legal challenges, if required; and
       •   Consultation with PAPs as part of effective RAP monitoring.
       •   Focus group discussions involving women only in project-affected communities
           (including host communities) to ensure that their views are taken into account, including
           their interests in terms of employment, skills and training. These consultation processes
           are designed to support equal participation of women (e.g., appropriate meeting times,
           transport & childcare support). They are being implemented by the female DLEOs,
           alongside Rogun JSC female gender focal points (for communities located within
           commuting distance of Rogun HPP).
       •   Focus group discussions and workshops for affected vulnerable groups, facilitated by
           the DLEOs, throughout the resettlement period. These are being hosted in community
           spaces that are accessible to all categories of vulnerable PAPs.
       •   MoLMEP employment fairs, which are taking place monthly throughout the construction
           period. These are hosted in liaison with Rogun JSC and the DLEOs, who will pro-
           actively engage with local businesses to identify job opportunities.
       •   Public Information Centers (PICs) have been established at the Rogun HPP site, as well
           as in the resettlement communities. The PICs are helping to facilitate stakeholder
           outreach, timely dissemination of information on project activities to affected and host
           communities and serve as grievance redress uptake and follow-up centers.


       11.4 Guide to Land Acquisition and Compensation (GLAC)
A GLAC has been developed for RAP 2, which informs communities of the approach to RAP 2 and
their rights. This is available in Russian and Tajik and is being shared by DFZ with all affected
communities. Photos of the GLAC are in Appendix C.
The GLAC includes the following information:
       •   A brief summary of the Project, timescales and land acquisition impacts;
       •   The standards and legislation used to prepare RAP 2;
       •   Stakeholder engagement arrangements;
       •   Current and potential Project impacts pertaining to land and resettlement;
       •   A summary of the resettlement process;
       •   Compensation entitlements and eligibility;
       •   Details of the resettlement-related GRM; and
       •   Channels to access further information.
The purpose of the GLAC is to provide a simple and accessible summary of key information
contained within the RLRF and RAP 2, to assist PAHs to be aware of their entitlements and rights.
An updated GLAC will be provided for each RAP period.

In 2024, the government of Tajikistan increased the amount of funding for PAH allowances and for
preferential loans.


112
In addition to the above, the project is undertaking the following commitments during
implementation of the resettlement program:
1. DFZ has been keeping careful track of the adequacy of compensation. In consultations with the
   World Bank between December 2022 and December 2024 involving some 200 households (out
   of a total of 1,710), about 90% of PAPs said they received sufficient compensation to build new
   houses and other structures. About 10% of resettled PAPs said they applied for additional
   compensation and most of them (about 80%) said they received additional financial and/or in-
   kind compensation, such as land, seeds, farming implements and support in getting local jobs
   or setting up small businesses.
   A separate survey of 364 resettled households in July/August 2023 (during the second phase of
   resettlement), indicated that 73% of PAHs reported that the financial compensation for their
   houses and structures – even with the permitted depreciation of 30% by the government – was
   adequate to purchase or build new houses and structures at the resettlement sites. Almost all
   PAPs agreed strongly that their new homes are superior in quality to their original homes, with
   important amenities such electricity and water supply, and upgraded or new schools and
   medical facilities to accommodate the resettled people as well as the host communities.
   The remaining 27% of PAH surveyed stated that they needed additional support, as materials
   and labor costs were higher than expected. This was especially the case from 2022 onwards
   due to inflation for critical building materials, such as wood and iron. Few of the PAPs who had
   resettled in earlier years raised concerns about the cost of building new homes. The resettled
   PAPs said they received additional support through family connections. It should be noted there
   was no data on how many of this group filed complaints through the GRM, but about half of the
   PAHs overall in the second phase of resettlement have complained through the GRM.
   The PAPs noted that DFZ also provided transportation of all their movable assets to the new
   resettlement sites, which helped minimize losses. The new houses were of higher quality, but
   generally slightly smaller than their original homes because the number of persons in the
   household was reduced, as married children received separate plots of land.
   As indicated above, on July 1, 2024, the government ceased applying the depreciation for
   household structures. By this time 778 households (7820 PAPs) had been compensated and
   resettled. As part of the ongoing consultations on the resettlement process, DFZ has been
   asking PAPs to come forward to the Grievance Redress Mechanism if their compensation was
   inadequate or resettlement remains incomplete due to the lack of amenities, such as water
   supply. DFZ will provide additional compensation, including in-kind support, to PAPs who
   demonstrate that the compensation resulting from the valuation of their houses and structures
   was insufficient for them to purchase or build new houses and structures sufficient to restore
   their living standards. To date, some 20 households have asked for additional compensation
   and most of the complainants have received additional compensation or in-kind support; some
   cases are in the process of being resolved.
   As for the households in the process of resettling, the survey indicates that 50% of the PAHs
   who are in the process of resettling have expressed concerns that their compensation may not
   be sufficient, not only due to the prices of construction materials. Another key challenge has
   been related to water supply to households: there is a problem with saline water in wells at
   some of the resettlement sites, which requires water to be provided by other means; and/or
   constructing water pipes has taken longer than expected due to challenges with terrain, water
   pressure, etc. Other concerns have been raised about construction quality for some houses and
   the timely construction of paved roads. Both resettled households and those in the process of
   resettling have noted that it takes time to restore livelihoods and income in the new resettlement
   areas.
113
2. The project aims to complete resettlement by 2032 but has built in some extra time if there are
   delays.
3. The resettlement lots have some flexibility in terms of the placement and orientation of the
   structures, but there are constraints due to the infrastructure related to electricity, water supply,
   sewerage and roads.
4. Fresh water supply is a challenge in some of the resettlement sites, given the presence of salt
   in the groundwater and/or the design of pipes with adequate water pressure. DFZ is working
   with relevant ministries and local authorities to build appropriate infrastructure to provide
   adequate water supply for drinking and irrigation.

5. For those families which have agricultural land, the project aims to make replacement land
   available near their homes, but, given the scarcity of suitable land in some places, land plots
   are sometimes made available to PAPs outside of their communities.

6. Small businesses may be able to re-establish in the new resettlement areas. Business owners
   are given equivalent land and can apply for preferential loans to set up their businesses. They
   also have the opportunity to undertake training to strengthen existing skills or learn new ones.

7. Some members of the communities at the end of the reservoir may not need to move from their
   current sites – if geological, hydrological, seismic and other data related to the safety and
   quality of land sites indicate that they can stay safely and have the requisite productive land
   they need for their livelihoods.

8. This RAP will, if necessary, include future provisions for adapting resettlement implementation
   in response to unanticipated changes in project conditions, or unanticipated obstacles to
   achieving satisfactory resettlement outcomes.
