13.    COSTS AND BUDGET
      13.1 Budget and Funding Arrangements
       13.1.1 Funding for Resettlement
The full cost of resettlement will be considered as a part of overall project costs, including adequate
contingency to cover unexpected circumstances or cost increases. This includes, but is not limited
to:
       •   Compensation at replacement cost for lost houses and other immovable assets;
       •   Compensation in kind for lost land, both residential and agricultural lands, including
           communal land for animals and/or trees;
       •   Cost of transport of all movable assets (including livestock) to the new site;
       •   Compensation for lost harvests, fruit trees or other produce;
       •   Compensation for the loss of access to resources which were in use by PAPs at their
           previous location (e.g., common land, pasture, woodland);
       •   Cost of livelihood restoration measures, including training in new farming techniques or
           skills;
       •   Compensation for lost income, per national law;
       •   Transitional assistance while income levels are restored, including targeted assistance
           for vulnerable persons;
       •   Costs of infrastructure development and public service capacity building at the new
           sites; and
       •   Additional funding that may be needed to fill past gaps in resettlement to date.
The details of RAP and LRP implementation budget, and source of funding for all resettlement
activities are included in this RAP, which includes an itemized budget for all individual activities,
staff time and training, provisions for inflation and monitoring costs. Funding will be from the
Government of Tajikistan and from external parties, such as international financial institutions.


       13.1.2 Budget for RAP 2/LRP 2
The total budget for resettlement activities taking place in 2017-2026 is calculated at 87.5 million
USD covering budgets of both resettlement and livelihood restoration activities. This includes:
       •   Asset compensation;
       •   Demolition work;
       •   Transitional allowance;
       •   Livelihood restoration programs, including training and apprenticeship programs;
       •   Resettlement site preparation - topographic-geodesic, geological surveys and design
           work;
       •   Administrative costs;
       •   Replacement infrastructure;
       •   Cemetery reburial;
       •   Information disclosure and engagement with PAPs;
       •   Legal advice and internal monitoring;

115
      •   External monitoring; and
      •   Contingency.

14.       GRIEVANCE REDRESS MECHANISM


   14.1 Establishment of the GRM
Grievances may be submitted verbally or in writing through a grievance form. Contact numbers for
DFZ representatives are shared with the public through information leaflets. If the grievance falls
outside the competences of DFZ, DFZ will assist PAPs to contact the relevant authorities in order to
resolve the complaint. PAPs may also seek redress through the national courts.
By law, grievances must be resolved within 30 days. The average close-out time is 10-15 days.
There is an appeals procedure in place if the problem cannot be solved in the first instance by DFZ.
According to a June 2024 survey of the PAHs yet to be resettled, the majority of non-displaced
households – 71% – indicated that they were aware of the procedures for filing a complaint or
requests/proposals under the Project.
In the same survey, the vast majority (82%) of resettled PAHs reported that they knew where to
turn in case of a request or complaint about the project. Seven percent reported that they had used
the more formal channels for filing grievances – either with DFZ directly or through the jamoats.
Another 35% said they had made fewer formal complaints to DFZ or government officials with other
ministries (e.g. Health, Education, MoLMEP and/or Agriculture).
DFZ began enhancing its GRM towards the end of 2023, first by reaching out to PAPs and second,
by centralizing and digitizing record-keeping.


      14.2        Updates and Enhancements to the GRM
During the second phase of resettlement, DFZ received 2458 oral and written complaints between
2017 and July 2025. 448 of these complaints were deemed not to be relevant, as they were not
related to the resettlement process68. DFZ handled the remaining 2,010 complaints themselves and
forwarded 325 to relevant competent authorities, such as the jamoats. All of the relevant complaints
were resolved, except for the following:
      •   11 complaints under consideration at the second tier.
      •   One complaint on land allocation which went to court and was eventually dropped.
Complaints have focused on the following issues:
      ▪   Replacement of land plots allocated to PAPs
      ▪   Amount of compensation; re-evaluation of the value of houses and other structures
      ▪   Allocation of land plots for commercial/retail use
      ▪   Assistance with documentation
      ▪   Allocation of communal land for specific purposes
      ▪   Requests for drinking water while water systems are being constructed
The vast majority of complaints are resolved by DFZ in coordination with other government
ministries and jamoats, but there are 20 which have been submitted for appeal (Tier 2). Nine had
been resolved and the remaining 11 are under consideration at the second tier.
The Project grievance procedure detailed in the SEP builds on the existing communication methods
and channels used by communities to raise grievances. The available channels are shown below.



68 Most examples related to commercial disputes or personal conflicts.

The two-tier process operates as follows:
Tier 1:
Step 1: Reporting:
All verbal and written complaints and grievances will be raised via the following channels:
          •       Grievance Mechanism Form (on paper):
              •    Stakeholders can fill grievance forms (see Annex 4 in the SEP) that will be
                   distributed by jamoats Heads and made available in affected settlements, DFZ
                   offices, affected jamoats offices and public venues (mosques, schools, libraries,
                   teahouses). The grievance forms can also be submitted in grievance boxes at these
                   locations.
          •       Face to Face:
              •    Stakeholders can voice their grievance to any DLEO, jamoats or to assigned DFZ
                   personnel at local offices.
          •       By telephone, post, email or SMS. The contact details are as follows:
              •    Phone: +992 37 235 74 16 or +992 37 235 74 17 Email: esia.rogun.hpp@gmail.com
              •    Post: Project Management Group for Energy Facilities Construction. 51 Shamsi Str.,
                   6th floor, 734064, Dushanbe, Tajikistan
Anonymous complaints can also be submitted to the GRM. A ‘complaint box’ has been placed in the
affected communities, DFZ offices and affected jamoats offices, and posters have been prepared
and displayed at key locations within the PACs in order to enable anonymous grievances to be
registered.
Step 2: Sort and Process:
Responsibility for managing community grievances sits with the DLEOs, supported by the
Community Engagement Manager (CEM) of DFZ. Affected community members can raise Project-
related grievances through the jamoats or directly to the DLEOs. All Project-related grievances
communicated to jamoats will be reported to the DLEOs, who are responsible for recording the
grievance in the logbook. The CEM will monitor and oversee this process.



120
The DLEOs categorize and sort the grievances. The DLEO determines the relevance of the
grievance, categorizes it, allocates the redress process, and assigns responsibilities. A broad
grievance categorization is used to ensure that the grievance management procedure is “fit-for-
purpose” and easy to use.
A centralized and digital grievance log has been developed and maintained by the DLEO, including,
as a minimum, the following information:
          •   Name and contact details of grievant (except for anonymous grievants);
          •   Date of grievance;
          •   Description of grievance;
          •   Categorization of grievance (DFZ to determine categories for monitoring purposes e.g.
              compensation payments, resettlement sites, environmental issues);
          •   Entity or individual (job role) responsible for grievance resolution, as determined by the
              DLEO;
          •   Resolution proposed;
          •   Date of close-out; and
          •   Close-out time (number of days).
The number of grievances in each category and close-out times is being monitored to identify
emerging or worsening issues.
The PMG Stakeholder Engagement Lead is responsible for collecting and managing grievances
from all other (non-community) stakeholders, such as NGOs or Riparian representatives. All
sensitive grievances, such as those relating to SEA/SH, are being directed to, and managed by a
Third-Party Sensitive GRM Officer. While complaints associated with the resettlement program, or
that are made by resettled people or host communities, are handled through DFZ, other complaints
are handled by PMG.
Further information on the specific roles and responsibilities associated with grievance
management is detailed in the SEP.
If the Tier 1 process does not resolve the grievance to the complainants’ or DFZ’s satisfaction, or if
broader consultation is necessary, the GRM will enter the external Tier 2 process.
Tier 2:
The Tier 2 resolution process involves a third party or parties. The Tier 2 process will be subject to
recognized national regulatory, cultural and independence requirements. The Tier 2 approach is
implemented on a case-by-case basis.
The grievant and DFZ must formally agree to enter the Tier 2 process, and on the participants to
the Tier 2 process. A third party should be neutral, well-respected, and acceptable to DFZ and the
grievant. The third parties may include public defenders, legal advisers, local or international
NGOs, technical experts, jamoats and village leaders, or any such acceptable institution. In the
case of a Tier 2 procedure, the DFZ legal team will be consulted.
On initiation of the Tier 2 approach, the grievant, DFZ and the third party will develop a roadmap
with associated timeframes, venues and associated procedures. The participants will determine the
nature of the process. This process can include facilitation, mediation, arbitration, resolution by the
third party, or any procedure deemed acceptable by the participants. There must be agreement on
the fees and payment aspects. Regarding the “no-cost” principle, grievances must be resolved at
no cost to the grievant. The Tier 2 process must be documented, transparent and inclusive.




121
After agreement on the roadmap and associated aspects, the Tier 2 procedure will be
implemented. The outcomes can lead to an agreement or fail to achieve a mutually acceptable
resolution.
If the outcome is acceptable to the grievant and DFZ, the Director and the grievant will complete
and sign a grievance resolution agreement. This action will close out the grievance and should be
logged as such.
The grievant or DFZ may choose not to accept the outcomes of the Tier 2 approach. In such a
case, DFZ or the grievant may seek other remedies, including those under the Tajikistan legal
framework. In this event, DFZ will terminate the grievance procedure and log the action as
terminated.




122
15. MONITORING AND EVALUATION
  15.1 Overview
Beginning in 2025 for RAP2, and from the commencement of each future RAP, there will be three
general types of monitoring: internal monitoring, external (3rd party) monitoring, and completion
audits.
  15.2 Internal Monitoring
DFZ currently carries out internal monitoring of all PAPs resettled to date, with separate data
collection in place for all households and families resettled today, disaggregated by the RAP that
they are covered by (RAP 1 or Phase 2 resettlement to date). The following indicators are
monitored for each resettlement site:
         •   Number of people
         •   Number of households and families
         •   Occupation of PAPs, broken down into the following categories:
         •   Children (<18 years);
         •   Home-maker;
         •   Labor migration;
         •   Unemployed;
         •   Worker;
         •   Retired;
         •   Student;
         •   Disabled; and
         •   Army.
This data is collected through district level representatives of DFZ on a monthly basis.
These arrangements are being expanded for the remainder of the RAP 2 period and future RAPs,
to include, inter alia, disaggregation by gender. Data will be reviewed annually to identify long-term
trends amongst the resettled communities. Monthly internal reports will be produced by DFZ ‘s E&S
monitoring team within the Resettlement and Livelihood branch of DFZ. The internal resettlement
monitoring report will share progress on each of the indicators defined in Table and will be shared
with PMG, MoLMEP and other key project stakeholders regularly. Internal monitoring reports will be
issued to the stakeholders for review and feedback on the 15th of each month, after the Director of
DFZ reviews and approves monthly monitoring reports. The Senior ESHS managers appointed at
PMG, MoLMEP and Ministry of Agriculture will have the opportunity to review and comment within
10 working days. Any actions that are proposed in the internal monitoring reports will need to be
shared with the relevant agency to state a timeframe to complete the action so that the gaps or
shortcomings can be fixed in a timely manner.

Internal monitoring will continue to be carried out monthly, and reports will be prepared quarterly by
qualified resettlement experts of the Project, or external consultants hired for the purpose. These
reports will be reviewed and signed by the Director or Deputy Director and shared with PMG for
information purposes. These reports will be made available to the external monitoring consultant
during annual monitoring and completion audits. The annual monitoring report will be disclosed.
The list of indicators will be reviewed and updated after each reporting round, and additional
indicators added if a need is identified.
   15.3 External Monitoring and Evaluation
Another layer of monitoring on top of internal monitoring will be external monitoring. External
monitoring will provide independent review on the ongoing resettlement and livelihood restoration
progress in line with the international requirements of the Project. DFZ will be responsible for
leading on the actions identified as necessary to remedy any shortcomings that might be identified
during the monitoring period. An independent third-party organization or consultancy is being
commissioned to undertake quarterly external RAP monitoring, as required by the Lenders and
international best practice, as well as annual evaluation of the resettlement program. This
organization will carry site visits and produce external reports assessing implementation of the
RAPs and LRPs. These reports will review and assess the ongoing progress, identify any gaps or
non-compliances with regards to the commitments in the RAP and LRP, and develop actions to
bridge those gaps. The monitoring organization’s assignment is based on Terms of Reference
(ToR) developed by DFZ (See Appendix I), which have been reviewed and approved by the
Lenders. DFZ is responsible for selection of the external monitoring organization, with approval
from the Lenders, who will ensure that the monitoring organization is suitably qualified, competent
and independent. This may be an NGO, private company, or other appropriate independent third
party.
   15.4 Completion Audit
When resettlement activities are completed for a given RAP, an independent third party
(Consultant) will be commissioned to undertake a Resettlement Completion Audit to confirm that
the resettlement and livelihood management program has been fully implemented in accordance
with the commitments under each relevant RAP and LRP. The external or independent third-party
consultant undertaking the completion audit will be hired by PMG and DFZ based on a Lender-
approved ToR, and the final selection of the Consultant will have to be approved by the Project
Lenders to ensure independence and competence. This Consultant will be independent from that
undertaking external RAP monitoring.
The completion audit will take place within one year of the completion of physical resettlement
under the given RAP. If non-compliances are discovered, time-bound corrective actions will be
proposed and included in an annex of the relevant RAP. The resettlement audit will be shared with
PMG and the Lenders for approval, and, once approved, will be made publicly available. DFZ will
bear overall responsibility for ensuring that the corrective actions are implemented, in cooperation
with other bodies with resettlement and livelihood activities (e.g., MoLMEP) if necessary.

APPENDIX C: LEGAL AND POLICY FRAMEWORK

National Legal and Policy Requirements on Land Acquisition and Involuntary
Resettlement
This section sets out the applicable national legal framework for the social aspects with regards to
implementing resettlement and livelihood restoration, access to information, public administration and
citizens’ rights. The 2025 ESIA chapter on the Administrative Framework includes a detailed list of the
applicable legislative framework related to the ESHS management of the Project.
Land and Resettlement
Table C-1: Legal requirements of the Republic of Tajikistan Regarding Land and Involuntary
Resettlement.

 Law                          Requirements
 Constitution of the          Article 12: ‘The economy of Tajikistan is based on various forms of ownership and the
 Republic of Tajikistan       State will guarantee freedom of economic activity, entrepreneurship, equality of rights, and
 (1994), as amended           the protection of all forms of ownership, including private ownership.’
                              Article 13: ‘The land, the mineral resources, the water, the air space, the animal and plant
                              kingdom, and other natural resources are the exclusive property of the State, and the
                              State guarantees their efficient use in the interest of the people.’
                              Article 32: ‘The property of an individual is taken away only on the basis of the law, with
                              the consent of the owner and to meet the requirements of the state and society, and with
                              the State paying full compensation.’
 Land Code of the Republic    Systematized set of rules regulating the rights to use, manage or lease land. Three types
 of Tajikistan (2006), as     of primary use are recognized:
 amended                      •    Perpetual use, which has no fixed term and can be granted to legal entities such as
                                   state bodies, industrial or transportation organizations, collective agricultural
                                   enterprises, and other such organizations.
                              •    Limited or fixed-term use, which can be granted to natural or legal persons for a
                                   short-term (<3 years) or long-term (3-20 years) period.
                              •    Life-long inheritable tenure, granted to natural persons or collectives. Natural persons
                                   must re-register upon receiving inheritance. This type of tenure covers dekhan farms
                                   and household plots.
                              •    The only secondary use/right recognized under the Land Code is the right to lease.
                                   Holders of primary use rights may lease their land for a period of up to 20 years,
                                   provided that the leased land is used in accordance with state-established land-use
                                   standards.
                              •    Seven categories of land use:
                                   •    Agricultural land;
                                   •    Urban and populated areas;
                                   •    Industrial, transport, and defense and other infrastructure;
                                   •    Natural, recreational, cultural and other protected land;
                                   •    Forest reserves;
                                   •    Water reserves; and
                                   •    State land reserves.
                              •    Amendments in 2012 allow the purchase, sale, exchange, gifting, and other
                                   modification of land rights.
                              •    Defines responsibilities.
                              •    Sets out the conditions upon land can be expropriated from land users for the needs
                                   of state projects, including the need to justify that there is no reasonable alternative.
                              •    Describes methods, systems, and order of protection of rights and interests of
                                   persons whose land is to be withdrawn.
                              •    Provides for compensatory measures to cover the land users’ losses, including the
                                   provision of equivalent land and structures and compensation for losses. This only
                                   applies to persons with legally recognized land use rights.

Law on Dekhan Farms 2016      •   This law is the legal basis for dekhan farms, which are individually, family or

                              •   The farmer(s) have inherited rights and may use the land as they see fit, but the State
                                  retains formal ownership and may repossess the land if they deem that it is not being
                                  managed well.

                                  may create a dekhan farm in groups of up to 50 people, and legal persons of any size

Law on Personal               •   Citizens have the right to personal subsidiary farming.

as amended                        bodies.


172
Law                             Requirements
                                •   Personal subsidiary farming is defined as ‘farming which is sustained by the personal
                                    labor of the citizen or members of their family for the purposes of agricultural
                                    production and the fulfillment of their needs regarding food and other necessities.
                                •   Personal subsidiary farming is not considered business activity.
                                •   ‘Family members’ are defined as those residing within one household.
                                •   Rural citizens have the right to receive land parcels for personal subsidiary farming
                                    from the Unified State Land Fund.
                                •   Urban citizens have the right to receive plots for lifetime inheritable use in line with the
                                    law of the Republic of Tajikistan.
                                •   Foreign citizens and stateless persons also have the right to receive land plots for
                                    personal subsidiary farming if free land is available.
                                •   Plots are to be provided by local authorities.
                                •   This law applies to both garden plots located next to residential houses and an
                                    additional plot from the Unified State Land Fund. The maximum size of such plots is
                                    legally defined.
                                •   Support is to be provided by state bodies for personal subsidiary farming, including
                                    expertise and credit.
                                •   When land plots used for personal subsidiary farming are requisitioned for state
                                    needs, citizens have the right to a replacement plot of equivalent quality, and to be
                                    reimbursed for all costs associated with the replacement of structures, physical move,
                                    and lost profits.
Law on Migration 1999           •   The Law on Migration defines the legal, economic, and social basis for internal and
                                    external migration, with the aim to regulate migration processes.
                                •   ‘Resettlers’ are defined as ‘people changing their place of residence due to the
                                    decision of a competent state body, not on an individual basis.’

                                         MIGRANT’S GUIDE

STATE INSTITUTION “DIRECTORATE OF THE FLOOD ZONE OF THE ROGUN HPP”


The State Institution “Directorate of the flood zone of the Rogun HPP” operates in accordance with the
requirements of the Government Decree No.47 of the Republic of Tajikistan dated January 20, 2009 “On the
resettlement of population of Rogun city and Nurobod district from the flood zone of the Rogun HPP” and ensures
phased implementation of the resettlement action plan and preparation of the Rogun HPP flood zone in
cooperation with the relevant ministries and agencies, executive bodies of state power of the resettling and host
cities and districts.

WHERE SHOULD MIGRANTS APPLY TO ON EMERGING ISSUES?

1. To the State Institution “Directorate of the flood zone of the Rogun HPP”:
    - On payment of compensation;

    - On the provision of the primary infrastructure facilities;
    - On potable and irrigation water supply;
    - On electricity supply;
    - On the provision with internal community roads;
    - Information about the master plan for building a school, kindergarten, building a health post and other
        types of infrastructure facilities.

        jamoats dehot)

     Republic of Tajikistan dated January 20, 2009 “On the resettlement of population of Rogun city and
     Nurobod district from the flood zone of the Rogun HPP”.

    -   After obtaining a homestead plot the migrant must deregister his/her passport from his initial place of
        residence and register it in the new place of residence.

    -   Together with the architecture and urban planning department of the host cities and districts to make
        sure that construction of a dwelling house is in line with the requirements of the master plan of the
        locality.

    -   Complete the construction of a dwelling house within the specified timeframe.

    -   Leave the flood zone in a timely manner.

    -   The migrants have the right to apply in the written form to the above indicated ministries and agencies
        to seek resolution of the existing issues.

    -   The migrant is obliged to submit necessary documents to the executive body of state power of the cities
        and districts to cover their children with education in their new place of residence.



179
   -   In addition, he/she is obliged to submit necessary documents of the disabled people and privileged
       persons to the relevant structures in a timely manner.

   -   Understand the purpose and implementation of the Decree No.47 dated January 20, 2009 and ensure
       its mandatory execution.

MAIN GOAL OF THE DIRECTORATE

Resettlement of project affected people, preparation of Rogun Hydropower Plant flood zone and in this regard,
it implements the following types of activities:
     - Resettlement of project affected people from the Rogun HPP flood zone.
     - Payment the value of the house, equipment, fruit trees, sown crops and other personal property in case
        of demolition.
     - Provision of trucks for transportation of the migrants’ property and belongings to the new place of
        residence.
     - Demolition of dwelling houses and cleaning of the forests, moving of facilities and buildings if needed.
     - Moving of cemeteries, historical and archeological monuments in line with sanitary and epidemiological
        requirements and environmental protection.
     - Moving of high voltage electricity lines, underground communication and etc., which prevent
        construction works in the flood zone.
     - Implementation of plans and measures and other aspects indicated in the organization’s Charter.


DIRECTORATE’S CONTACT DETAILS:
10, Bokhtar Street, Dushanbe city, 734025
Tel: 227 9403; 227 94 11
Fax: 227 9425
E-mail: mdmmz@interent.ru

                                 DECISION OF THE ROGUN CITY CHAIRMAN



No.119

Rogun city                                                                        September 27, 2023



                       On the Approval of the State Commission Act of Acceptance
         of the completed construction of the private houses of the citizens in Yoli Garmoba village,
                                    Qadi Ob jamoat dehot, Rogun city


        In accordance with Article 20 of the Constitutional Law of the Republic of Tajikistan On the Local
Bodies of State Power, Articles 72 and 75 of the Urban Planning Code of the Republic of Tajikistan, I hereby
decide:
        1. To approve in general the State Commission’s Act of Acceptance of the completed construction
            of the private houses of the citizens in Yoli Garmoba village, Qadi Ob jamoat dehot, Rogun city
            (according to the enclosed list of the resettled people from the Rogun HPP flood zone) with the
            value of the main assets, construction area, useful area and total area, which are indicated in the
            technical passport;
        2. To assign to the residents of Yoli Garmoba village (according to the enclosed list) the buildings
            with completed construction as private houses, which were accepted by the State Commission;
        3. To obligate the Director of the SSE “Registration of the immovable property” in Rogun city to
            ensure state registration of the dwelling houses of Yoli Garmoba village residents (according to
            the enclosed list) and provide Certificate on State Registration.
        4. To send for information and implementation this decision to the Tax Inspectorate in Rogun city,
            architecture and urban planning department, section of the Statistical Agency in Rogun city, Qadi
            Labi Ob jamoat dehot of Rogun city and residents of Yoli Garmoba village.
        5. The first deputy chairman of Rogun city, the chairman of land management committee and
            architecture and urban planning department shall be responsible for monitoring the
            implementation of this decision.

                                                  Chairman

222
APPENDIX H: SUMMARY OF FOCUS GROUP DISCUSSIONS (FGDs) AND
CONSULTATIONS

First-round FGDs were carried out with a total of 43 women from communities undergoing or
awaiting resettlement in Lughuri Poyon (Sicharogh jamoats), Hakimi (Hakimi jamoats, Nurobod),
Mehrobod (Mehrobod jamoats, Nurobod), and Kachimandi (Shahrisabz) / Furudgoh (Nusratullo
Makhsum jamoats, Rasht) and Navobod (Navobod jamoats, Rasht), on 8th-10th July 2023. FGDs
with a total of 35 men were also carried out in Sicharogh and Khakimi jamoats (pre-resettlement
communities) and at a resettlement site in Faizobod.
Second-round FGDs were carried out with men and women from communities at all resettlement
stages, as well as host communities, in Khumdon (Nurobod), Darband (Nurobod), Sicharogh
(Rogun), Khurramdara (Rogun), and I. Halimov (Nurobod).
The participants were found to be generally optimistic about the Project and the opportunities that
will be available for them after resettlement. This was confirmed by post-resettlement participants,
and by those who had talked to friends and family who had already moved. However, issues were
identified regarding compensation calculation, water supply in resettlement sites, and, in some
settlements – particularly in Rasht – lack of clear information about resettlement. Men and women
were found to hold similar views about the Project and resettlement, although, for women, their
level of agency within the process was found to vary greatly between individuals.

and contracting organizations, suppliers, as well as design research institutes to ensure timely
implementation of the systematic process of resettlement and socio-environmental activities in the area,
implementation of production plans in 2024.




241
APPENDIX I: PAST RESETTLEMENT AND LIVELIHOOD RESTORATION

Past Resettlement and Livelihood Restoration Implementation
The resettlement process began with the initiation of Project construction in the 1980s, and a ban
on building or improving dwellings in the villages marked for resettlement was put in place. The
resettlement process was discontinued while the Project was paused in the 1990s and 2000s,
during which time many people who had been resettled returned to their previous residences.
However, the ban on new construction and major alterations remained in place.
It is expected that the completion of the Project will require the resettlement of up to 69 inhabited
villages. Geological studies are ongoing in 12 of these villages to confirm whether resettlement will
be required. Current estimates state that approximately 46,000 people will be resettled over the
entire course of the Project.
The resettlement operation can be divided into several phases:
       •   The initial Soviet-era resettlement phase in the 1980s, which was somewhat reversed in
           the 1990s.
       •   Phase 1, which took place in 2008-2017 and is already complete. This was implemented
           in line with the initial 2014 RPF (RPF 1) and Resettlement Action Plan (RAP 1) prepared
           in line with World Bank standards.
       •   Phase 2, which began in 2017 and is ongoing. This phase is much larger, covering an
           estimated 1,421 households, and is estimated to be completed by 2025. The
           resettlement process in this phase is relying on the 2014 RAP and RPF 1, which will be
           updated to become this RLRF and 2017-2026 RAP 2.
       •   Later phases, taking place 2025-2032, which will be managed by future RAPs. It is
           estimated that a total of 5 RAPs will be required.
During all resettlement phases to date, compensation for assets was determined by means of the
THP prepared for each plot containing an inventory of assets, which is signed by the HoH, and a
subsequent valuation of these assets. Land compensation was provided in the form of a
replacement plot selected by lottery at the resettlement site chosen by the HoH. RAP 1 required
compensation to be paid at replacement cost. A completion audit carried out in 2018 confirmed that
living standards had improved.
A completion audit of RAP 1 was carried out by Baker Tilly in 2018. It confirmed that relocation was
complete, compensation at replacement cost had been paid to all PAPs, and that average living
conditions had improved substantially, in terms of size and quality of dwellings, availability of
infrastructure, and, in the case of PAPs relocating from rural to more urban areas, employment
opportunities. All resettled household heads had rebuilt their houses, however the construction of
houses on land plots that had been given to families (adult children who were not HoH, alongside
their spouses and children) that previously resided within other households was still ongoing, as
these families did not own properties in their former locations and therefore did not receive
compensation. New houses, in both rural and urban resettlement sites, exceeded constitutional
minimum requirements 12 m2 per person, which had not been the case at previous locations.
Infrastructure and services including healthcare, schools, access to electricity, and availability of
drinking water were confirmed to have improved by surveyed PAPs.
School attendance amongst children, particularly amongst girls, was found to have improved, and
180 women had begun working outside the home in their new location, 40 of whom had benefited
from DFZ-provided training.
The completion audit found that, of the resettled population, 28% were in paid employment, 20%
were running small businesses, 45% were engaged in seasonal labor migration, 2% in seasonal
domestic work, and the remaining 5% had other income streams. Data on their previous
242
occupations however was not available. It was confirmed that PAPs had been enabled to use
previous land plots until all crops had been harvested. Approximately 90% of resettled PAPs were
found to grow food in garden plots at their new locations.
The completion audit found that 40% of affected adults (predominantly men) had sought training in
new agricultural techniques, and also that 180 women who had not worked outside the household
were now economically active (social work, small businesses, crop production, seasonal work).
Two groups of vulnerable PAPs were identified and entitled to the following resettlement
assistance:
            •    Female-headed households (7 PAPs);
            •    Jamoats to prioritize provision of labor;
            •    Relocation assistance (packing, unpacking, salvaging material from previous
                 dwelling);
            •    Construction monitored by jamoats; and
            •    Additional construction materials provided;
            •    People with disabilities (16 PAPs)
            •    As above, as well as:
            •    Provision of wheelchairs if needed (4 PAPs);
            •    DFZ-constructed public facilities and infrastructure, as well as reconstructed houses,
                 adapted to be accessible for the disabled; and
            •    Assistance to register with labor and social protection agencies in order to receive a
                 special monthly pension, as well as compensation and assistance related to
                 resettlement.
Vulnerable PAPs (defined as those being at risk of exclusion from livelihood restoration activities
and those requiring customized approaches to livelihood restoration such as the disabled and
elderly) were entitled to priority consideration for Project-related employment, and to be prioritized
for employment opportunities through local authorities.
Regular monitoring by a Tajik NGO, Kumak, was carried out, most recently in 2019. Internal
monitoring by DFZ is also carried out monthly at resettlement sites, covering the number of PAPs
and households and families resettled and their occupations.
LRP 1
A LRP was developed alongside RAP 1 in 2015, in line with national legislation and OP 4.12, with
the aim of restoring the livelihoods and living standards of Phase 1 resettled PAPs to at least their
pre-resettlement levels. It was estimated that an additional 3-5 years would be required for the
completion of this LRP, beyond the completion of physical resettlement.
This LRP contained the following key information:
        •       Background information on the Project and Phase 1 resettled communities;
        •       Legal basis of the LRP;
        •       Supporting data from RAP 1 and RPF 1;
        •       A breakdown of positive and negative impacts on livelihoods resulting from resettlement;
        •       Identification of livelihood restoration opportunities at resettlement sites;
        •       Livelihood restoration measures provided by MoLMEP;
        •       Identification of potential partners to support livelihood restoration implementation;

243
       •   Eligibility criteria, including for vulnerable groups entitled to additional assistance;
       •   Description of entitlements and EM;
       •   Implementation Plan;
       •   Consultation, disclosure and GRM;
       •   Institutional arrangements, roles and responsibilities;
       •   Monitoring and reporting; and
       •   Estimated budget.
LRP 2 has been prepared as a separate document. This RAP includes a summary of it.

APPENDIX J: TERMS OF REFERENCE – INDEPENDENT MONITOR FOR
RESETTLEMENT ACTIONS PLANS/LIVELIHOOD RESTORATION PLANS


1. Introduction

The Project Management Group for Energy Facilities Construction Under the President of the Republic of
   Tajikistan (PMG) is coordinating development of the Rogun Hydropower Project (HPP), which is under
   construction on the Vakhsh River about 100km east of Dushanbe. The dam has been under construction
   intermittently for decades and is projected to be complete by about 2032, The reservoir will be impounded
   in stages and at its maximum, which will be initially reached by about 2039, will extend about 80km
   upstream and cover about 170km2.

Over 50,000 people in 77 villages will be physically displaced by the rising reservoir. The resettlement
   program is managed by the State Enterprise, Directorate of Flooding Zone of Rogun HPP (DFZ) and is
   following a phased approach aligned with the reservoir flooding.

Phase 1 resettlement covered eight villages and 2,697 project-affected people between 2008-2017 and was
   declared complete in a resettlement completion audit (2018) approved and disclosed by the World Bank.

The remaining phases of resettlement cover the remaining 69 villages and is being managed through multiple
   resettlement and livelihood restoration plans. The ongoing second phase covers the resettlement of 16
   villages and almost 17,000 people between 2017-2026. A Resettlement Action Plan 2 (RAP 2) and
   Livelihood Restoration Plan 2 (LRP 2) will be in place by early 2025 and will meet the updated
   requirements of international lenders, including the World Bank Environmental and Social Standard 5.

Resettlement in subsequent phases will be outlined in RAPs 3, 4, and 5 in future years.

RAP2/LRP2 and subsequent Plans for the Rogun Hydropower Project (HPP) will involve three general types
  of monitoring: internal monitoring, external (3rd party) monitoring, and completion audits. The present
  terms of reference are focused on external (3rd party) monitoring and is to be based on the indicators used
  for tracking the implementation and performance of the RAPs/LRPs (see below).

Internal Monitoring Process
DFZ is currently carrying out internal monitoring of all project-affected people (PAPs) who have been
   resettled to date, with separate data collection in place for all households and families. The following
   indicators are monitored for each resettlement site:

    Number of people
    •   Number of households and families
    •   Occupation of PAPs, broken down into the following categories:
        a.   Children (<18 years);
        b.   Homemaker;
        c.   Labor migration;
        d.   Unemployed;
        e.   Worker;
        f.   Retired;
        g.   Student;
        h.   Disabled; and
        i.   Army.




246
This data is collected through district level representatives of DFZ on a monthly basis. Commencing with RAP
    2, the data also being disaggregated by gender. The data may also be broken down in other ways, such as
    by age, in order to track any key trends in the resettlement/livelihood restoration process. The data will be
    reviewed annually to identify long-term trends amongst the resettled communities.

Monthly internal reports are being produced by DFZ’s environmental and social (E&S) monitoring team
  within their Resettlement and Livelihood branch. The internal resettlement and livelihood restoration
  monitoring report will share progress on each of the indicators defined in Table and Table below. These
  indicators have been outlined in the Resettlement and Livelihood Restoration Framework (RLRF).
  Additional indicators may be added as and when required. The reports will be shared with PMG, Ministry
  of Labor, Migration and the Employment of Population (MoLMEP) and other key project stakeholders
  regularly. Internal monitoring reports will be issued to the stakeholders for review and feedback on the 15th
  of each month, after the Director of DFZ reviews and approves the monthly monitoring reports. The Senior
  Environmental, Social and Health and Safety (ESHS) manager appointed at PMG, as well as counterparts
  at MoLMEP and the Ministry of Agriculture will have the opportunity to review and comment within 10
  working days. Any actions that are proposed in the internal monitoring reports will need to be shared with
  the relevant agency to state a timeframe to complete the action so that the gaps or shortcomings can be
  fixed in a timely manner.

Internal monitoring will continue to be carried out monthly, and reports will be prepared semi-annually by

    this ToR. These reports will be reviewed and signed by the DFZ Director or Deputy Director and shared

    during annual monitoring and completion audits.

The list of indicators in Tables 1 and 2 will be reviewed and updated after each reporting round, and additional
   indicators added if a need is identified.

2. Objective

The purpose of these Terms of Reference is to define the requirements for external monitoring of the
   resettlement and livelihood restorations programs for Phase 2—that is, for RAP 2 and LRP 2, which will
   be disclosed for public review and comment in early 2025. The external monitoring will provide an
   independent review on the ongoing resettlement and livelihood restoration progress in line with the
   international requirements of the Project. DFZ will be responsible for leading on the actions identified as
   necessary to remedy any shortcomings that might be identified during the monitoring period.

Each RAP and LRP will have a monitoring section to detail the timing of hiring the external monitoring
   organization, and this is expected to occur immediately after the RAP/LRP has been adopted. If needed,
   the TOR for monitoring will be updated based on the experience of the previous effort.

It is possible the same external monitoring organization will be used for all the RAPs and LRPs, but the current
     appointment will be for RAP 2/LRP 2.

3. Scope of Work for External Monitoring and Evaluation of the RAP 2/LRP 2 Programs

As required by international Lenders and good international industry practice, PMG, in consultation with DFZ
    is commissioning an independent third-party organization or consultancy to undertake quarterly RAP/LRP
    monitoring and semi-annual reporting, as well as annual evaluation of the resettlement program.


250
The major objectives of the RAP/LRP monitor are to:

         (i)         Assess the overall approach of resettlement activities in the project area;

         (ii)        Assess whether actions and commitments defined in the RAP or LRP are implemented fully
                     and delivered on time;

         (iii)       Verify results of internal monitoring;

         (iv)        Assess whether resettlement objectives have been met; specifically, whether livelihoods and
                     living standards have been restored or enhanced;

         (v)         Assess whether complaints and grievances are being followed up with appropriate corrective
                     actions and tracking of outcomes;

         (vi)        Assess that vulnerable persons are tracked and assisted as necessary75;

         (vii)       Assess resettlement efficiency, effectiveness, impact and sustainability drawing lessons as a
                     guide to future resettlement policy making and planning;

         (viii)      Identify recurrent or systemic problems that require correction; and

         (ix)        Ascertain whether the resettlement entitlements are appropriate in meeting the objectives, and
                     whether the objectives are suited to the PAPs.


Taking into account the monitoring indicators in Tables 1 and 2, the responsibilities of the RAP/LRP monitor
   will include (but not be limited to) the following:

    Carrying out quarterly independent monitoring at the resettlement sites hosting resettled PAPs and villages
        undergoing resettlement for the given RAP. This will consist of two visits by the full team per year,
        plus no fewer than two visits by the locally based team member in between the visits of the full team.
        Any reports or observations by the locally based team member will be included in the semi-annual
        reporting;
    Formulating additional indicators or KPIs for monitoring based on the first semi-annual report of the
       monitoring team tasks to help improve data collection and continuous improvement in the process;
    Monitoring the payment of compensation, ensuring that the correct amount has been delivered to the
      PAPs, and that the payments are received within the agreed timeline;
    Assessing the sufficiency of compensation provided and the effectiveness of livelihood restoration
       measures (i.e. satisfaction with dwellings and satisfaction with income restoration measures);
    Interviewing a random sample of PAPs to assess their experiences with the resettlement and livelihood
        restoration measure. These could include:
                 o   Focus group discussions with various categories of affected households or individuals such as
                     women, youth, elderly, disabled or other vulnerable groups to assess the impacts of the
                     resettlement on such groups and how the implementation can be made more responsive to
                     meet their needs, especially the most vulnerable;
                 o   Consultations with a sample of people who have registered grievances to assess if the
                     grievance process is being implemented effectively and grievances addressed in a timely
                     manner, as well as assessing trends and types of grievances that are being recorded;


75 As indicated in Table 1 above, data on vulnerable groups should be disaggregated from the PAPs as a whole, with information on the

   gender of the heads of families.
251
            o   Discussions (in the form of key informant interviews or focus group discussions) with district
                and village leaders; and
            o   Discussions with any civil society groups involved in livelihood restoration activities, or who
                established or presented themselves during the implementation process.
    Reviewing grievances recorded by DFZ through the District Livelihood and Engagement Officers
       (DLEOs) and assessing the effectiveness of the grievance mechanism;
    Number of open grievances at the end of each quarter and trends over time;
    Identifying shortcomings with regards to the relevant RAPs/LRPs and proposing actions to improve
        performance, as well as making recommendations for optimization of the resettlement process in line
        with evolving best practice; and
    Preparing monitoring reports – to be made available in Tajik, English, and Russian – for submission to
        DFZ, PMG and the Lenders.

19. The RAP/LRP Monitor may formulate additional indicators for monitoring, based on the first monitoring
    report to help improve data collection and continuous improvement in the process

20. The RAP/LRP monitor will also examine the following qualitative aspects of resettlement/livelihood
    restoration:

    Level of PAP knowledge about resettlement procedures and entitlements;
    Understanding by PAPs on fulfillment of the RAP/LRP provisions;
    Sufficiency of time and budget to meet objectives of the respective RAPs/LRPs;
    Adequacy of support to vulnerable groups;
    Management of unforeseen problems by DFZ and other State entities involved in resettlement/livelihood
       restoration; and
Other issues, such as unintended environmental or social impacts. The RAP/LRP monitor will also prepare
   evaluation reports at the end of the year that provide:

        •   Overall trends in resettlement process as per the monitoring findings;

        •   Assessment of progress on RAP implementation;

        •   Overall spending on resettlement activities and distribution of spending on relevant items
            (examples): Planning and surveys, Cash compensation or in-kind compensation, Resettlement site
            development and housing, Livelihood restoration, Vulnerable groups, General implementation
            services, including engagement and grievance management, and overheads;

        •   Issues and challenges in restoring project-affected households’ physical dwellings or restoration of
            livelihoods;

        •   Impacts on vulnerable groups (e.g. have some households become vulnerable due to illness or
            death of the main wage earner or loss of their support networks); and

        •   Satisfaction with community facilities (drinking water, power, community center, school, health
            clinic, recreational facilities, access roads, etc.).

Each report will be prepared initially in draft, revised draft, and final versions, with subsequent versions
   revised in response to comments from PMG, DFZ, Lenders, and other parties.

5. Proposal Instructions

Technical proposals will include the following sections and may be no more than 25 pages long, excluding
   CVs in an annex:

    Introduction to the organization, consultancy, or other category of entity that proposes to do the work and
        any subcontractors/sub consultancies: No more than two pages.
    Key personnel: description of the role to be filled by the experts and a brief (up to half-page) description of
       qualifications and experience.
    Technical approach (up to 10 pages), including methods and approaches to semi-annual monitoring and
       end year evaluations. Details should also be provided regarding the experts who will lead and provide
       major support. Methodology and approaches should include (but not be restricted to)
            a. Collection of field-level information in order to allow review and comparison of the
               socioeconomic condition of PAPs.
            b. Evaluation of efficiency, effectiveness, impact and sustainability based on collected
               information on key indicators of the resettlement progress.
            c. Methods for at least one post-evaluation surveys to assess the achievement of resettlement
               objectives, the changes in living standards and livelihoods and the restoration of the
               economic and social base of the PAPs.
            d. Process by which the monitoring program will:
               Review project baseline data on income and expenditure, occupational and livelihood
                   patterns, leadership, community organizations and cultural parameters;
               o Review results of internal monitoring and verify claims through random checking
                   at the field level to assess whether resettlement/livelihood restoration objectives
                   have been generally met;
               o Involve PAPs and community groups in assessing the impact of resettlement
                   programs for monitoring and evaluation purposes; and
               o Conduct both individual- and community-level impact analysis through the use of
                   formal and informal surveys, key informant interviewers, focus group discussions,
                   community public meetings, and in-depth case studies of PAPs, including
                   vulnerable groups.

            e. The RAP/LRP monitor may also wish to undertake Participatory Rapid Appraisals
               (PRAs). PAPs and host communities, as well as local civil society organizations should be
               involved in the monitoring and evaluation process to solve day-to-day problems arising
               during resettlement operations. PRA techniques could be undertaken to foster the
               involvement of PAPs and other key stakeholders in resettlement monitoring and
               implementation. The methodology of the PRA should be as follows:


253
             Key informant interviews: select local leaders, village workers, local organizations,
                 women’s groups or persons with special knowledge or experience about
                 resettlement activities and implementation;
             Focus group discussion: specific topics (e.g., land compensation payments, services at
                 resettlement sites, income/livelihood restoration, and gender issues);
             Field observations on status of resettlement/livelihood restoration implementation,
                 plus individual or groups interviews for cross-checking purposes; and
             Informal surveys/interviews: informal surveys of PAPs, hosts, village workers,
                 resettlement staff, and implementing agency personnel using non-sampled
                 methods.
    Past performance: project descriptions of up to five projects completed by the key experts and/or the
        organization that describe the work, identify the client, list the key experts who will support the current
        project, and provides contact information for a client contact if additional information is needed. Each
        description may be up to one page.
    CVs for key experts: no more than three pages each.

The appointment is expected to be made by the end of Q1 2025 and continue through at least 2027, with the
   possibility of extension. Financial proposals will define lump sum costs to complete the work. Financial
   proposals will identify labor and other direct costs. Proposals may break costs down by tasks as desired but
   will include total costs per report, with invoicing based on deliverables. It should be assumed that
   monitoring may be extended until at least 2035.

7. Selection and Appointment of the RAP/LRP Monitor

27. In consultation with DFZ and subject to acceptance by the Lenders, PMG will select the external
    monitoring organization whose proposal is most responsive to the solicitation, meets the qualifications
    described below, and deemed to be the most cost-effective. The successful contractor may be a civil
    society organization (CSO), nongovernmental organization (NGO), private company, or other appropriate
    independent third party. The monitor is expected to be a small team of professionals involving one or more
    entities, and it is strongly encouraged that there be strong Tajik support, including training for local
    support personnel.

    The monitor for the third phase of resettlement will be selected once RAP3 and LRP3 are prepared and
    adopted.
